==PAGE:3==
Summary (English)
This thesis investigates a dynamical model describing a chromatographic sepa-
ration process based on an ideal equilibrium relation, and proposes numerical
models for accurate and eﬃcient simulation of this process. Algorithms for state
estimation of the chromatographic process are developed, and these algorithms
are based on the proposed numerical models of the underlying process. The
content of this thesis naturally divides into separate parts.
The ﬁrst part introduces and develops two numerical models for simulation
of chromatographic processes: 1) a high-order, discontinuous-Galerkin ﬁnite-
element (DG-FE) model and 2) a standard low-order, ﬁnite-volume (FV) model.
The FV method is developed to serve in comparison with the proposed DG-
FE method. The results from this comparison illustrate the capability of the
high-order method to produce accurate solutions at low computational eﬀort.
Compared to the FV method, the DF-GE method is able to generate solutions
at much higher accuracy, while using an order of magnitude fewer states. This
makes the DG-FE method an ideal simulator.
The second part investigates algorithms for state estimation of linear chromato-
graphic processes. A disturbance model is proposed for simultaneous estimation
of the uncertain states and inlet concentration of the chromatographic pro-
cess, using the Kalman ﬁlter as an optimal linear estimator. Parallel to the
results from the ﬁrst part, the DG-FE method is able to produce state esti-
mates with higher accuracy and lower computational eﬀort than the the FV
method. These improvements are again by an order of magnitude, and makes
the DG-FE method ideally suited for state estimation.
==PAGE:4==
ii
The third, and ﬁnal part, investigates four algorithms for state estimation of non-
linear chromatographic processes. The four ﬁlters are the: 1) Extended Kalman
Filter (EKF), 2) Ensemble Kalman Filter (EnKF), 3) Unscented Kalman Filter
(UKF) and 4) Particle Kalman Filter (PF). A comparison of the four nonlinear
ﬁlters reveals, that the EKF is the most computationally feasible, while being
capable of producing reasonably accurate state estimates. A deterministic and
probabilistic approach towards nonlinear input estimation is given by a com-
parison between the EKF and EnKF, and both ﬁlters are capable of estimating
the uncertain inlet concentrations using an augmented model formulation of the
nonlinear model.
The work during this project gave rise to the writing and submission of three
conference papers. These papers are listed in the following:
1. A Discontinuous-Galerkin Finite-Element Method for Simulation of Packed
Bed Chromatographic Processes (submitted and accepted).
2. Spatial Discretization and Kalman Filtering for Ideal Packed Bed Chro-
matography (submitted).
3. State and Input Estimation of Nonlinear Chromatographic Processes (sub-
mitted).
Each paper has been enclosed in the thesis in Appendix C - E.

C h a p t e r 1
Introduction
Chromatography was ﬁrst developed in Russia by the Italian born Mikhail
Tswett, as a preparative technique for separating plant pigments such as chloro-
phyll, carotenes, and xanthophylls, at the turn of the last century [GFSK06].
The word chromatography originally derives from Greek chroma, which means
to color and graphein, which means to write. Since these plant pigments have
diﬀerent colors, the technique was given its name. The discovery made in the
ﬁrst decade of the 20th century brought with it a growing interest in chromatog-
raphy. An interest which has seen active and steady increase, to a point where
chromatography is now acknowledged as a ubiquitous industrial unit operation,
for the extraction and puriﬁcation of ﬁne chemicals, many which are used in the
pharmaceutical and biotechnology industry [GB11].
1.1
The Chromatographic Process
The chromatographic separation process may be performed in both small and
large scale. Small scale production is commonly referred to as analytical chro-
matography, while large scale production is commonly known as preparative
chromatography. As an analytical technique, chromatography is used to identify,
analyze and quantify diﬀerent compounds of a given mixture. The analytical
==PAGE:16==
2
Introduction
technique may be used to evaluate parameters of the diﬀerent components, or
used as a preliminary stage to preparative chromatography [HM16]. In produc-
tion and industrial scale however, column liquid chromatography is widely used
as an important unit operation for downstream processing of biopharmaceutical
products. Preparative chromatography is used in chemical and pharmaceutical
production in order to isolate and purify compounds of interest [GB11].

Chromatographic Column
Mobile phase
Figure 1.1: Conceptual representation of the molecular separation that occurs
in a packed bed chromatographic column.
Figure 1.1 shows a conceptual representation of the molecular separation, which
occurs inside the column of a packed-bed chromatograph. The column is packed
with a ﬁne grained porous material, where a continuous ﬂow of liquid containing
a mixture of many diﬀerent components is injected through a nozzle at the inlet
into the column.
This continuous ﬂow forms what is known as the mobile
phase in the column. As illustrated by concept in Figure 1.1, this procedure
creates a ﬂow, which carries molecules of each component in the mobile phase
through the column from left to the right using either gravity or pressure. The
physical and chemical properties of each component and the porous medium
lead to diﬀerent aﬃnities towards each other, and molecules transition from
the mobile to stationary phase by adsorption onto the porous medium. The
speed of this transition is dependent on these aﬃnities. This interaction between
components inside the column, is used as a way to separate the molecules, where
they move along the column at diﬀerent speeds, forming diﬀerent concentration
bands that may be extracted at the outlet. Typically, a known solute with a
==PAGE:17==
1.1 The Chromatographic Process
3
strong aﬃnity to the stationary phase is injected to manipulate pH, polarity or
another property that may inﬂuence the separation of the molecules of interest.
The inﬂuence of this solute may be used to control the chromatographic process,
and steer the process towards a desired state. Eﬃcient control may in turn lead
to an increase in yield of the desired components, and reduce the costs of the
chromatographic process.
Figure 1.2 shows a High-Performance Liquid Chromatograph (HPLC) stationed
in building 227 at the Technical University of Denmark. Figure 1.3 shows an
actual chromatographic column, which Figure 1.1 is a conceptual representation
of.
The column has a length of 15 cm, and is a small scale column, which
may be operated by the chromatograph. Columns such as this one has many
applications. It can be used to analyze samples from fermentations, determine
the secondary metabolite concentrations in given medium and to determine
speciﬁc yields of fermentations.
Figure 1.2: A High-Performance Liquid Chromatography unit stationed in
building 227 at the Technical University of Denmark.
==PAGE:18==
4
Introduction
Figure 1.3: A chromatographic column around 15 cm is shown, which may be
used in conjunction with the chromatographic separation unit.
1.2
Literature Overview
While chromatography is extensively used as a separation technique in down-
stream biopharmaceutical manufacturing, practical application of chromatogra-
phy remains a costly process [HSJ+18]. In 2015 the global biopharmaceutical
market was predicted to reach 167 billion US Dollars [GB11]. As a consequence
the potential mathematical modeling, parameter estimation, control and opti-
mization may have on chromatographic processes is therefore becoming increas-
ingly signiﬁcant.
Accurate modeling and simulation of chromatographic processes described by
models with increasing complexity [ABFN18, HLv18, vA10, KDE00, BWA+13],
is an active and extensively studied ﬁeld of interest. Open modeling frameworks
such as the Chromatography Analysis and Design Toolkit (CADET) [Lv18]
provide freely distributed software under the GPL license capable of executing
the most common scientiﬁc workﬂows such as model calibration, process design,
experimental design as well as many other designs of chromatographic processes.
Parameter estimation [KE06, KDS+09a, LNE14, KDS+09b], optimization [PSNv13,
HM16, Sel18] and control [Sel18] try to bridge the gap between minimizing the
high cost associated with the chromatographic process, while maintaining an
eﬃcient separation with a high production yield.
1.3
Motivation and Challenges
The success of the chromatographic separation process is tied to the fulﬁllment
of two objectives: 1) to extract as much as possible of each component from
==PAGE:19==
1.3 Motivation and Challenges
5
a given mixture, 2) with each component separated as much as possible and
in the least amount of time. To optimize the separation process, it therefore
becomes natural to consider appropriate control strategies such as Nonlinear
Model Predictive Control (NMPC).
The fulﬁllment of these two objectives inherently pose a number of diﬀerent
challenges, among these challenges it is notable that: 1) the dynamics of the
chromatographic processes are inherently described by complex models, and
these complex models often require independent determination of many un-
certain model parameters.
These parameters may be be costly and diﬃcult
to derive by experiments [GFSK06, CSBS14, KE06, KDS+09a]. In addition,
these models are described by nonlinear, convection-dominated partial diﬀeren-
tial equations (PDEs), that require specialized numerical methods in order to
be solved accurately and eﬃciently. 2) Measurements of the inlet concentrations
of each component are typically not possible to obtain for industrial feed stocks,
and information about the process is therefore tied entirely to the uncertain mea-
surements taken at the outlet [BWA+13]. The measurements available at the
outlet are typically not of the concentration, but have to be computed indirectly
through available data obtained by the speciﬁc process used for separation.
However as a fundamental prerequisite, NMPC and other control techniques
rely crucially on proper state estimation of the underlying process. A conceptual
illustration of the NMPC principle is shown in Figure 1.4. This ﬁgure depicts
the exchange of information between each part of an NMPC loop.
During
discrete times, tk, the chromatographic process provides the estimator with
measurements, y, which are obtained using sensors. The estimator computes
the ﬁltered states, ˆx, and the regulator is used to dynamically optimize the
process with a speciﬁed control u.
To meet the above challenges and provide the ﬁrst steps towards NMPC of
chromatographic processes, this project seeks to develop fast and accurate nu-
merical methods, that upon discretization of the coupled nonlinear PDEs lead
to numerical schemes, that ensure high-order accuracy using few degrees-of-
freedom (DOFs). Furthermore, this project seeks to develop reliable state es-
timation techniques of the underlying chromatographic processes. To this end,
this project falls into two main parts.
1.3.1
Fast Simulation of Chromatographic Processes
The simulation of multi-component chromatographic processes under nonlinear
conditions and equilibrium based kinetics require fast and accurate numerical
methods [JSAW11, HLv18, MHA18b]. These processes are described by non-
==PAGE:20==
6
Introduction
Estimator
Regulator
Process/Model
y
x
u
v
Figure 1.4: Conceptual representation of the interaction between state esti-
mator, regulator (dynamic optimizer) and the process/model in
NMPC. This project seeks to develop an accurate numerical model
of the chromatographic process and eﬃcient state estimation al-
gorithms. These components constitute the monitoring and pre-
diction part of the NMPC loop.
linear, convection-dominated coupled PDEs [JSAW11]. The underlying physics
of these PDEs are convection-dominated, and discontinuities in the inlet con-
centrations may over time give rise to steep concentration proﬁles and shock
formations on either side of the elution bands [MG92]. These shocks challenge
and cause diﬃculties for many numerical methods based on ﬁnite-diﬀerence or
ﬁnite-volume discretizations, and to mitigate this, eﬃcient high-order accurate
schemes are necessary to produce reliable simulations of the underlying physical
models [MHA18b].
The ﬁrst part of this thesis seeks to mitigate the numerical diﬃculties posed to-
wards conventional numerical schemes, by using a high-order, nodal discontinuous-
Galerkin ﬁnite-element (DG-FE) scheme, as an accurate and performance eﬃ-
cient simulator [MHA18b]. This numerical scheme oﬀers h-p adaptivity, which is
attractive since high-order accuracy may be achieved by increasing the number
of elements for a ﬁxed polynomial order or by increasing the polynomial or-
der for a ﬁxed number of elements [HW08]. The Equilibrium Dispersive Model
(EDM) is proposed as nonlinear model to describe the dynamics of the under-
==PAGE:21==
1.4 Contributions
7
lying chromatographic process.
1.3.2
State Estimation of Chromatographic Processes
The inlet concentrations of the chromatographic column often consist of a mix-
ture of many diﬀerent and complex molecules. These inlet concentrations typi-
cally result from a fermentation in the downstream biopharmaceutical manufac-
turing process. Most often it is not possible to obtain measurements of the inlet
concentrations of each component, that enters the chromatographic column.
Therefore information of the process is tied entirely to the measurements taken
at the outlet, which only begin to appear after the chromatographic process is
undertaken. As a consequence, information of the concentration at the outlet
of the chromatographic column need to be computed indirectly from available
measurements, and this data is typically corrupted by measurement noise.
This second part of this thesis seeks to investigate the simulation of linear
and nonlinear chromatographic processes under uncertainty.
The discretiza-
tion of the EDM leads to a stochastic, continuous-discrete model. This project
will develop state estimation techniques for simultaneous estimation of the sys-
tem states and unknown inlet concentrations of linear and nonlinear chromato-
graphic processes. The Kalman ﬁlter is used as an optimal estimator for the
linear chromatographic process, while the following four diﬀerent ﬁlters are used
as estimators for the nonlinear process. The ﬁlters considered are the: 1) Ex-
tended Kalman Filter (EKF), 2) Unscented Kalman Filter (UKF), 3) Particle
Filter (PF) and 4) the Ensemble Kalman Filter (EnKF).
1.4
Contributions
The work during this project gave rise to three conference papers. The project
was carried out in the period from August 25th 2018 to January 25th 2019, and
the papers were written and submitted, while simultaneously working on the
project. In the following, the three papers are listed in publication order as:
1. A Discontinuous-Galerkin Finite-Element Method for Simulation of Packed
Bed Chromatographic Processes, which has been submitted to the 12th
IFAC Symposium on Dynamics and Control of Process Systems (DY-
COPS), and has currently been accepted.
==PAGE:22==
8
Introduction
2. Spatial Discretization and Kalman Filtering for Ideal Packed-Bed Chro-
matography, which has been submitted to the European Control Confer-
ence 2019 (ECC).
3. State and Input Estimation of Nonlinear Chromatographic Processes, which
has been submitted to the IEEE Conference on Control Technology and
Application (CCTA).
1.5
Thesis Outline
The thesis consists of the following chapters:
• The Chromatographic Model: This chapter introduces the EDM used
for modeling the chromatographic process. The model is derived using
an equilibrium mass balance consideration.
The choice of equilibrium
relations describing the transition from mobile to stationary phase, which
couples the dynamics between the components occupying the process, are
outlined in this chapter as well.
• The Discontinuous Galerkin Method: The mathematical prelimi-
naries for understanding the DG-FE method are presented.
Based on
this understanding, this chapter derives a spatial discretization using a
method-of-lines approach, which considers the spatial and temporal dis-
cretization of the EDM separately. The high-order, DG-FE method is used
to discretize the spatial derivatives of the model. In addition, a ﬁrst-order,
FV method is derived to be used in comparison with the DG-FE method.
Three propositions on how to obtain solutions to the nonlinear model are
presented, and an eﬃcient time-step procedure based on a Low-Storage
Explicit Runge-Kutta (LSERK) method is proposed to advance solutions
to the nonlinear EDM in time. An exact time-integration is derived for the
linear EDM. Finally, an array of diﬀerent case studies are investigated in
order to demonstrate the capabilities of the DG-FE method, as an ideally
suited simulator.
The main work of this chapter gave rise to the ﬁrst conference paper
paper: A Discontinuous-Galerkin Finite-Element Method for Simulation
of Packed Bed Chromatographic Processes.
• State Estimation of Nonlinear Chromatography: This chapter be-
gins by developing a stochastic, state-space model, that results from the
discretization of the linear EDM using the DG-FE and FV method. This
model assumes, that the noise present in the process is kept constant over
==PAGE:23==
1.5 Thesis Outline
9
each discrete time interval. The dynamic and stationary Kalman ﬁlters
are presented and described as estimators of the stochastic state-space
model. Furthermore, a stochastic, continuous-discrete model, is proposed
to model the nonlinear chromatographic process under uncertainty. The
EKF, UKF, PF and EnKF are described for the numerical model that re-
sults from the discretization of the nonlinear EDM. A disturbance model
is proposed to model the unknown inlet concentrations of the chromato-
graphic column. The Kalman ﬁlters are applied in the linear and nonlinear
cases as estimators, and are used to estimate the uncertain inlet and outlet
states simultaneously. The capabilities of the DG-FE method for ﬁltering
is demonstrated using both the linear and nonlinear model.
The main work of this chapter gave rise to the two remaining conference
papers: 1) Spatial Discretization and Kalman Filtering for Ideal Packed-
Bed Chromatography and 2) State and Input Estimation of Nonlinear
Chromatographic Processes.
• Outlook: Conclusions are made in this chapter, and recommendations
for future work are given.
==PAGE:24==
10
Introduction
==PAGE:25==
C h a p t e r 2
The Chromatographic
Model
The following chapter presents the theoretical foundation for understanding the
mathematical model of chromatography used in this thesis.
The content of
this chapter is divided into separate parts. The ﬁrst part introduces three fun-
damental models of chromatography.
These models are the: 1) Equilibrium
Dispersive Model (EDM), 2) Lumped Kinetic Model (LKM) and 3) General
Rate Model (GRM). The second part consists of an assessment and discussion
of the complexity of each mathematical model, and based on these consider-
ations the EDM is chosen as an adequate model. The third part provides a
derivation of the EDM based on a mass balance equilibrium. Finally, an analyt-
ical solution is presented to the EDM based on a linear equilibrium relation of
the concentrations. The analytical solution will prove useful later in the thesis,
as it provides a way of comparing the accuracy of numerical schemes used to
approximate solutions of the model. It also ensures that these numerical models
are implemented correctly.
==PAGE:26==
12
The Chromatographic Model
2.1
The Diﬀerent Models of Chromatography
The literature contains a variety of models, that describe the behavior of packed-
bed chromatographic processes inside a separation column. Well known models
[GFSK06] include the:
1) Equilibrium Dispersive Model.
2) Lumped Kinetic Model.
3) General Rate Model.
Other models such as the ideal and lumped pore model exist, but this section is
dedicated to describing the three most common models: 1) through 3). These
models are nonlinear, convection-dominated PDEs describing the interaction
between chromatographic components inside a separation column. The models
1) through 3) are derived from mass balances, each under varying assumptions.
The EDM is considered to be the simplest of the three models, and is based on
six fundamental assumptions [GFSK06]. These assumptions are: 1) the chro-
matographic process is isothermal, and the transition from mobile to stationary
phase is modeled by an equilibrium isotherm with instantaneous reaction ki-
netics. 2) The mobile phase is incompressible. 3) There is axial dispersion in
the column, which causes band broadening of the concentration proﬁles.
4)
The stationary phase is packed with a porous material of equidistant spherical
particles, and the solvent is not adsorbed in the stationary phase. 5) The col-
umn is radially homogeneous and 6) the mobile and stationary phase of each
component do not interact.
The Equilibrium Dispersive Model: Under the following assumptions 1)
through 6) the EDM is governed by the dynamics of the nonlinear, convection-
dominated PDE on the form:
∂ci
∂t + v ∂ci
∂z = Di
∂2ci
∂z2 −F ∂cs,i
∂t ,
(2.1a)
cs,i = fi(c1, c2, . . . , cNc).
(2.1b)
The ith concentration of the mobile phase and stationary phases are denoted
respectively as ci and cs,i. The parameters of the EDM (2.1) are listed as:
1. v the interstitial velocity of the ﬂuid.
2. Di the ith axial diﬀusion coeﬃcient.
==PAGE:27==
2.1 The Diﬀerent Models of Chromatography
13
3. F the dimensionless phase ratio.
4. ϵ the dimensionless total porosity of the packing bed material inside the
column.
The EDM typically appears on the form (2.1), however this is an inconvenient
representation of the model, for reasons that will become apparent later in the
thesis. A more convenient representation is introduced, which eases the physical
understanding and implementation of the conservation based numerical schemes.
The equilibrium relation describing the transition from mobile to stationary
phase is governed by the term (2.1b). This relation implies that the reaction
kinetics are instantaneous, and that equilibrium of the mobile and stationary
phase is achieved immediately. This is a reasonable assumption in most cases,
since component concentrations in nonlinear chromatographic processes remain
relatively low, with concentrations rarely exceeding 10% (w/w). A variety of
models exist for describing the relation in (2.1b), such as the linear and Langmuir
isotherm models. An extensive list of these models are given in [GFSK06].
The EDM is correct if the mass transfer in the chromatographic separation col-
umn is governed solely by molecular diﬀusion across the mobile phase ﬂowing
around the packing material, and if the exchange of feed components between
the stationary and mobile phases is very fast. Otherwise, the model is an ap-
proximation. However, because most modern chromatographic columns achieve
high eﬃciency using ﬁne-particle packing materials, the model is often a realistic
approximation of the true dynamics inside the column [GFSK06].
The Lumped Kinetic Model: The dynamics of the LKM are governed by
the same PDE (2.1), as in the EDM. However, the mass balance equation in
(2.1) is combined with a kinetic equilibrium relation, which relates the rate of
variation of the concentration of the ith component in the stationary phase, to
its concentrations in each mobile phase and to the equilibrium concentration in
the stationary phases. This gives a model on the form:
∂ci
∂t + v ∂ci
∂z = Di
∂2ci
∂z2 −F ∂cs,i
∂t ,
(2.2a)
∂cs,i
∂t
= gi(c1, c2, . . . , cNc, cs,1, cs,2, . . . , cs,Nc; k),
(2.2b)
cs,i = fi(c1, c2, . . . cNc).
(2.2c)
In other words, the equilibrium is no longer achieved instantaneously as in the
EDM, but rather the equilibrium is attained after some period of time has
passed during the chromatographic process.
The speed of the transition to
equilibrium between each concentration phase is governed by the kinetic mass
transfer coeﬃcient k.
==PAGE:28==
14
The Chromatographic Model
Diﬀerent kinetic models exist to model the dynamical equation in (2.2b), such
as the linear kinetic and Langmuir kinetic model. These models are listed ex-
tensively in [GFSK06]. In principle, the kinetic models are more exact than the
equilibrium based ones, such as the EDM. However, the diﬀerence between the
band proﬁles produced from the EDM and the LKM are negligible when the col-
umn eﬃciency is high and exceeds a few hundred theoretical plates [GFSK06].
The dynamics of the LKM is therefore captured by the simpliﬁed EDM for high
eﬃcient chromatographic columns.
The General Rate Model: The GRM is the most comprehensive and real-
istic of the three models, since it takes into account all of the phenomena that
inﬂuence the formation of the band proﬁles inside the chromatographic column.
The GRM considers separately two fractions of the mobile phase: 1) the fraction
that percolates through the column bed, which carries the components along the
column denoted denoted ci, and 2) the stagnant fraction contained in the porous
particles denoted cp,i. A mass balance equation is written for each of the two
phases for each of the i components. The dynamics of the GRM are described
by a model on the form:

The concentration qi denotes the stationary phase concentration at equilibrium.
For the EDM the stationary concentration, cs,i, equals the stationary concen-
tration at equilibrium, qi, since the mass transfer kinetics occur instantaneously.
The GRM contains a large collection of parameters. However, for completeness
these parameters are listed below as:
1) ap is the external surface area of the packing material.
2) ci and cp,i are the bulk and stagnant ith mobile phase concentration.
3) cs,i is the ith stationary phase concentration.
4) qi is the ith stationary phase concentration at equilibrium.
5) Di is the axial dispersion coeﬃcient of the ith component.
6) Dp,i is the pore diﬀusion coeﬃcient of the ith component.
7) Ds,i is the surface diﬀusion coeﬃcient of the ith component.
==PAGE:29==
2.1 The Diﬀerent Models of Chromatography
15
8) ϵc is the external porosity of the column bed.
9) ϵp is the internal porosity of the packing material.
10) kext,i is the ﬂuid particle mass transfer coeﬃcient.
11) r and rp are the radial position and radius of the spherical particles.
12) v is the interstitial velocity of the ith component.
The GRM is a radially heterogeneous model, since there is friction between
the occupying components and the wall surrounding the column. Therefore the
packing density, phase ratio, permeability, external porosity, interstitial velocity
etc. are diﬀerent depending on the radial and spatial coordinates (z, r). The
GRM considers surface diﬀusion, Ds,i, pore diﬀusion, Dp,i, which introduces the
radial dimension and axial diﬀusion, Di, which causes band broadening to the
particles in the column. This is contrary to the EDM, which does not assume an
inﬁnitely eﬃcient column, and instead lumps all the dispersive eﬀects into one
dispersion coeﬃcient Di. Finally, the GRM models the eﬀective rate constant
kext,i by a ﬁlm transfer coeﬃcient.
While the GRM is the most comprehensive and realistic model of chromatogra-
phy, since it takes into account many of the phenomena that have inﬂuence on
the formation of band proﬁles, it is however also the most complex model. The
GRM requires determination of many of the aforementioned parameters, which
may not be readily available. Typically, these parameters have to be determined
by parameter identiﬁcation through a series of experiments. While this may be
acceptable in practical cases, it is often not easy, since this requires the acqui-
sition of accurate band proﬁle data in a wide range of experimental conditions.
This is both expensive and time consuming, and explains why the GRM is not
as popular as its more simple counterparts the EDM or LKM [GFSK06]. Use of
the GRM should therefore be limited to cases, where the mass transfer kinetics
of the components are slow.
In speciﬁc cases [QAJ+13, GFSK06, van81] when either of the the physical mod-
els (EDM, LKM and GRM) simplify to a linear model by choice of equilibrium
and kinetic relation, it has been shown that closed form solutions to these mod-
els exist. These closed form solutions are not useful for simulation of the real
chromatographic processes, as the conditions and assumptions on which they are
derived typically reﬂect unrealistic simulation scenarios. In order to accurately
model the dynamics of the EDM, LKM and GRM, eﬃcient numerical schemes
that guarantee solutions, even when the models are complex and nonlinear, need
to be considered. Based on the latter discussion, the model of scope in this the-
sis is chosen as the EDM. This is a relatively simple model, which accurately
predicts the formation of band proﬁles, and for high eﬃciency chromatography
==PAGE:30==
16
The Chromatographic Model
the EDM is a good approximation of the behavior inside the chromatographic
column. However, in order to model the chromatographic process appropriate
boundary conditions, and information about the equilibrium isotherm relation,
needs to be speciﬁed. These considerations are made in the subsequent section.
2.2
The Equilibrium Dispersive Model of Chro-
matography
The EDM is a nonlinear, convection-dominated PDE of the form (2.4a) and
involves the concentrations of Nc components in the mobile, ci, and stationary
phase at equilibrium, qi.
In the literature, a variety of boundary conditions exist to model almost any
conﬁguration of the column [GFSK06]. A Danckwerts boundary condition is
used to model the inlet concentration proﬁle, and a zero Neumann condition
is given at the outlet [Dan53]. For high-performance chromatographic systems
with many theoretical plates, the diﬀusion at the inlet is almost zero and a
Dirichlet condition is typically used to model the inlet concentration. Given
proper initial conditions, the multi-component ﬂow inside the column is gov-
erned by the nonlinear, convection-dominated PDE on the form:

equation (2.18). The inﬂux of mass is typically modeled by a Robin boundary

condition, while for D > 0 the boundary condition accounts for dispersive ef-
fects at the inlet, which in theory states that particles of each component may
disperse back into the pipe inlet.

2.4
Equilibrium Isotherms for the EDM
The equilibrium isotherm is an equilibrium relation between the components oc-
cupying the chromatographic column, which describes the willingness of a com-
ponent to transition from mobile to stationary phase and stick to the porous
medium. The EDM is derived based on the assumption, that the concentra-
tions of the ith component in its mobile and stationary phase are assumed to
be equilibrium at all times. In other words, the reaction kinetics of the chro-
matographic process are assumed to be instantaneous. The equilibrium relation
typically relates all the components occupying the chromatographic column, and
this relation in general implies that qi is a function qi = fi(c1, c2, . . . , ci, . . . , cNc),
which depends on all the components in the chromatographic column. There-
fore f is typically a nonlinear function, that couples the equations in (2.4), and
this transition may therefore be described by a variety of equilibrium isotherms,
which models qi.
The chromatographic literature oﬀers a variety of diﬀerent equilibrium isotherms
for both single- and multi-component systems [GFSK06]. In general, the amount
of any given component adsorbed at the equilibrium state within a mixture al-
most always decreases with the increase of the amount of another component. A
collection of diﬀerent components in the mobile phase which are occupying the
chromatographic column, will therefore always experience an inherent competi-
tion to occupy the space in the stationary phase. This behavior is not captured
by one single expression for the equilibrium isotherm, but rather a variety of
diﬀerent isotherms exist. These relations are each based on diﬀerent assump-
tions, and are useful for modeling diﬀerent phenomena. One such equilibrium
model is the multi-component Langmuir isotherm.
The Multi-Component Langmuir Isotherm
The following section derives the multi-component Langmuir isotherm. Consider
two components A and B of a binary mixture at equilibrium. Depending on
their aﬃnity towards each other and the porous material they occupy, either of
==PAGE:36==
22
The Chromatographic Model
the components in the mixture may stick to a particle of the porous medium, and
occupy a fraction of the surface. One part, S0, of the surface is free, implying
it is only covered by the solvent, one part, SA, is covered by the molecules of
component A and the remaining part, SB by the molecules of component B.
Thus the total surface covered is:
S0 + SA + SB = 1,
(2.24)
where S0, SA and SB is the surface area covered by the solvent and components
A and B.
At equilibrium the rate of adsorption equals the rate of desorp-
tion. The rate of desorption is proportional to the surface area occupied by
the molecules of the speciﬁc component [GFSK06].
During equilibrium this
proportionality is given as:
kd,i = ka,iS0Ci,
= ka,iCi(1 −SA −SB).
(2.25)
The subscript i denotes, which of the components are considered. The coeﬃ-
cients kd,i and ka,i are the desorption and adsorption rates for the speciﬁc com-
ponent. Equation (2.25) gives two equations with two unknowns for i ∈{A, B}.
Solving these simultaneously gives:
Si =
ka,i
kd,i Ci
1 + ka,A
kd,A CA + ka,B
kd,B CB
.
(2.26)
The amount of the ith component that is adsorbed, i.e in stationary phase qi,
is proportional to the surface area covered by the molecule. This derivation
was given for a binary mixture, but for systems with Nc > 2 components the
expression generalizes. Instead, one may deﬁne what is known as the competitive
Langmuir isotherm for a mixture of Nc components, by considering a sum of
areas S0 + PNc
j
Sj = 1. The competitive Langmuir isotherm is then given as:

The equilibrium coeﬃcients in (2.27) are simply the fractions of the adsorption
and desorption coeﬃcients in equation (2.26).
The Linear Isotherm
In dilute systems where concentrations are very low, the competition between
components become negligible, and the equilibrium relation described by the
Langmuir isotherm (2.27) simpliﬁes to a linear isotherm:
qi = aici.

2.5
The Linear EDM
The following introduces the linear EDM, and the closed form solution to the
governing PDE of this model is presented. Given a linear equilibrium relation
(2.28) the EDM (2.4) reduces to a linear PDE given as:

1 + 1 −ε

(2.29)
under the same boundary (2.20) + (2.22) and initial conditions.
2.5.1
Solution to the Linear EDM
The choice of equilibrium isotherm determines, how the dynamics of the diﬀerent
components described by the EDM will behave. The Langmuir isotherm couples
the EDM such that it for Nc > 1 becomes a system of multiple PDEs. In most
cases of multi-component chromatography, it is not possible to give any general
statements of the dynamics described by the coupled equations of said process
[GFSK06]. In almost every case one has to rely on numerical simulations to
give a description of the dynamics of the chromatographic process. In speciﬁc
simulation scenarios and under simplifying assumptions, it has been shown that
closed form solutions to the EDM (2.4) do exist.
However, for systems described by the linear isotherm, the PDE of the EDM
decouples, such that the dynamics for each component are independent. Given
a single component system with an equilibrium relation described by the linear
isotherm it is possible to obtain a closed form solution to the EDM using a
Danckwerts and Neumann boundary at the in- and outlet. The exact solution
is derived in the Laplace domain, and back transformation to physical time is
possible, providing an expression for the solution. The solution is derived in
[van81, QAJ+13] and is on the form:
c(t, z) =
(

0 < t ≤tinj,

(2.30)

Closed form solutions such as (2.30) are interesting for a variety of reasons, and
may be used to study isolated eﬀects of the chromatographic system. However,
in the context of this work the solution will be used later to prove the convergence
rates of the DG-FE and FV schemes, since it can be used to provide accurate
estimates of the numerical approximation error.
While closed form solutions exist for the linear EDM, no such forms exist for
the nonlinear EDM. The lack of such solutions, emphasizes the need for numer-
ical schemes, that accurately and in an eﬃcient way, are able to compute the
dynamics of the coupled equations governing the nonlinear model. Therefore,
the purpose of the following chapter is to derive an accurate and eﬃcient nu-
merical scheme, which is based on the DG-FE method to solve the EDM when
equilibrium relations in the process are nonlinear.
==PAGE:40==
26
The Chromatographic Model
==PAGE:41==
C h a p t e r 3
Discretization by the
Discontinuous-Galerkin and
Finite-Volume Method
The following chapter covers the theoretical background, implementation and
results of the discontinuous-Galerkin ﬁnite-element (DG-FE) and ﬁnite-volume
(FV) method. The ﬁrst part begins by providing an understanding of the pre-
liminaries of each method. The actual discretization of the EDM by both nu-
merical methods are described in detail afterwards. Finally, the implementation
of the numerical methods, and results of this implementation, are covered in the
remaining sections.
The model equations in (2.4) are discretized using a method-of-lines approach.
This procedure allows for a separate discretization of the spatial and temporal
derivatives. The spatial derivatives are discretized using the DG-FE and FV
method [MHA18b], and this results in a continuous-time discrete-space model.
The DG-FE method is chosen, since it oﬀers high-order accuracy and h-p adap-
tivity i.e. reﬁnement of the approximation can be done by either increasing the
number of elements for a ﬁxed polynomial order (h-adaptivity) or by ﬁxing the
number of elements and increasing the polynomial order (p-adaptivity). The FV
method is chosen as a simple model to benchmark against the DG-FE method.
Given a linear equilibrium relation a state-space model is obtained from the
==PAGE:42==
28 Discretization by the Discontinuous-Galerkin and Finite-Volume Method
continuous-time discrete-space model by an exact time integration. However,
for the nonlinear equilibrium relation an explicit Runge-Kutta method is chosen
as a time-step procedure, and Newton’s method is used to solve the governing
nonlinear equation (2.5) during each time-step.
3.1
The Galerkin Approximation
Galerkin (Spectral) methods and ﬁnite-element methods are closely related.
Both methods originate as series solutions to PDEs. Spectral methods approxi-
mate solutions to PDEs by ﬁnite series of orthogonal basis functions. Common
choices for such basis functions are for example the complex exponential func-
tions, Chebyshev or Legendre polynomials. However, Hermite, Laguerre and
other orthogonal polynomial series may also be considered as expansion func-
tions.
Finite-element methods approximate solutions to PDEs by piecewise
linear functions.
The main diﬀerence between each method is that spectral
methods use globally deﬁned basis functions, while ﬁnite element methods use
locally deﬁned basis functions. By combining the concepts of both methods, one
may arrive at a broader class of methods known as the spectral ﬁnite-element
methods. The DG-FE method is an example of a method, which combines these
two approaches. To develop a spectral ﬁnite-element method, there are three
fundamental choices that need to be considered. These choices are:
1) How to determine what local orthonormal basis functions should be used
on each element.
2) How to compute nodes on each element.
3) How to determine the solutions unknowns of the method.
The remaining part of this chapter will be spent on developing the DG-FE
method for solving the EDM, using the principles described in 1) through
3). The mathematics presented in the following sections are based on [Kop09,
HGG07, Lea15].
3.2
The Normed Approximation Space
The following introduces the appropriate normed approximation space, which
contains the relevant basis functions used to construct the DG-FE method.
==PAGE:43==
3.3 The Choice of Orthogonal Basis Functions
29
The approximation space is equipped with its inner product and norm, and is
the weighted Hilbert space L2
w(a, b). The weighted Hilbert space L2
w(a, b) has
support on [a, b] where a, b ∈R, and is deﬁned as:
L2
w(a, b) =
(
f :]a, b[→R,
Z b

(3.1)
The inner product associated with this space is:
(f, g)L2w(a,b) =
Z b

(3.2)
for ∀f, g ∈L2

with the inner product, and is deﬁned as:
||f||L2w(a,b)=

(3.3)
The following sections mainly considers a subsection of this space L2
w(−1, 1).
3.3
The Choice of Orthogonal Basis Functions
A deﬁning character of any spectral method is the choice of orthogonal basis
functions used to approximate the solution with. For periodic domains a natural
choice is to approximate the solutions with a Fourier basis, which considers the
complex exponentials [Kop09] in a series expansion:
FN(x) =
N
2
X
k=−N
2
ˆfkeikx,
(3.4)
for some order N. This approximation is a modal expansion with expansion
coeﬃcients ˆfk. A modal expansion is an expansion in terms of a set of basis
functions and corresponding expansion coeﬃcients as in (3.4).
The periodic
nature of this representation is not well suited for problems of non-periodic
nature, such as the PDEs described by the EDM (2.4). The periodic nature of
the Fourier basis functions, does therefore not ﬁt well with the framework of
the DG-FE method, and based on this consideration a more appropriate basis
is chosen later.
When the solutions are non-periodic alternative basis functions should be con-
sidered, such as the orthogonal polynomial basis functions mentioned previously.
A nodal approximation is a representation of orthogonal polynomials as an in-
terpolant through a set of node values. Both a nodal and modal approximation
may be used to construct the DG-FE method. However, in this context, a nodal
DG-FE method is used.
==PAGE:44==
30 Discretization by the Discontinuous-Galerkin and Finite-Volume Method
3.3.1
The Polynomial Basis Functions
Spectral methods are typically represented in terms of polynomial series, and
an eﬃcient way of generating orthogonal polynomial basis functions is therefore
necessary. One way to generate these polynomial bases is by use of the Strum-
Liouville Theorem [Kop09]. The main result of the theorem states that under
certain assumptions, the solutions or eigenfunctions of the corresponding Strum-
Liouville Problem form a basis for the weighted space L2
w(a, b) introduced in
Section 3.2 . These eigenfunctions are therefore useful, to represent solutions
that are square integrable.
On the interval (−1, 1) only a distinct class of polynomials satisfy these as-
sumptions. They are known as the Jacobi polynomials, and are represented by
P α,β
k
(x). The order of the polynomial is k and α, β > −1. These polynomials
are orthogonal with respect to the weighted inner product of L2
w(−1, 1) that is:
Z 1
−1
P (α,β)
l
(x)P (α,β)
k
(x)w(x) dx = γ(α,β)
k
δlk.
(3.5)
These polynomials form a basis in L2
w(−1, 1). Furthermore, these polynomials
are eﬃcient for approximating smooth problems, as they will converge at a
rate depending on the smoothness of the functions being expanded, without
the need for any special conditions such as periodicity. The ﬂexibility of these
polynomials make them ideal for constructing spectral ﬁnite-element methods
to approximate solutions on non-periodic domains.
The Jacobi polynomials are easy to evaluate, diﬀerentiate and integrate, and
in general they oﬀer attractive properties, that make them ideal for eﬃcient
numerical implementation: 1) The Jacobi polynomials may be computed by a
three term recurrence, which makes them computationally eﬃcient to imple-
ment, and ideal for the development of the spectral methods. 2) For k ≥1, the
polynomials P (α,β)
k
have k distinct real roots in the interval (−1, 1). This feature
is useful when evaluating discrete inner products using Gauss quadratures. 3)
Finally, the derivatives of the polynomials also satisfy a three term recurrence
relation.
Two series of polynomials exist, which are of particular usefulness to spectral
methods. These polynomials are the Legendre polynomials:
Lk(x) = P (0,0)

(3.6)
and the Chebyshev polynomials:

Both polynomials have attractive features, that may tailor the numerical method
in a speciﬁc way. The Legendre polynomials have weight functions w(x) = 1,
and the corresponding inner products are easily evaluated.
The Chebyshev
polynomials have attractive approximation properties as they may be evaluated
fast using the Fast Fourier Transform [Kop09]. A more rigorous discussion of
these choices is presented in [Kop09].
3.3.2
The Legendre Polynomials
The Legendre polynomials are a particular class of Jacobi polynomials. These
polynomials will be extensively used in this thesis, and as such they are presented
more thoroughly in the following. They are obtained as eigenfunctions to the
Strum-Liouville problem when α = β = 0, are polynomials in L2(−1, 1) since
w(x) = 1 and satisfy the three term recurrence:

The convergence of this approximation is clearly dependent on the decay rate of

k=0. In fact the convergence rate may accurately
predicted for any function f ∈Hp

w(−1,1),
(3.22)
for any real valued constant C.
If the function is not smooth, that is p = 1, then the approximation converges
slowly at a ﬁrst-order rate. However, if the function is inﬁnitely smooth, that is
p = ∞, the approximation converges rapidly towards the exact solution. This
property is known as spectral accuracy, and it highlights a strong feature of
the spectral methods. Since for arbitrarily smooth functions f, that is p = ∞,
the polynomial series converge at an exponential rate towards the true solution.
The spectral convergence property provides a fast and accurate approximation,
using only a few degrees of freedom (DOFs) compared to other approximation
methods, but also requires suﬃcient smoothness of the solution.

The matrices M and Dr constitute the fundamental building blocks of the
DG-FE method. These matrices may in principle be computed directly from
any chosen basis. However, in this particular case an orthonormal Legendre
basis is chosen. These building blocks allow for the discretization of the EDM
by the DG-FE method, and they provide a way for computing the solution
unknowns in the DG-FE method. This completes the requirement in the third
paragraph in Section 3.1. Based on these considerations the details of the DG-
FE discretization of the EDM are covered in the following section.

3.4.3
Imposing the Boundary Conditions
The DG-FE method allows for a practical implementation of the boundary
conditions, which is done weakly by deﬁning the exterior ghost states (c+, g+) at
==PAGE:61==
3.5 Global Assembly of the DG-FE and FV Method
47
the left and right most boundary of Ω. These terms are introduced as part of the
numerical ﬂux, but are not well-deﬁned on the boundary of the domain. They
are naturally deﬁned as part of the implementation of the boundary conditions.
Begin by considering Danckwerts condition in (2.4b) given as:
p = vc −D ∂c
∂z = vcin.
(3.83)

f(c−) + f(c+)

(3.84)

(3.85)

(3.86)

3.5
Global Assembly of the DG-FE and FV Method
The following section covers the global assembly of the high-order DG-FE and
ﬁrst-order FV method. Typically, a local assembly of the numerical methods
as in [MHA18b, HCM+19] would be preferred, since it allows for a fast and
==PAGE:62==
48 Discretization by the Discontinuous-Galerkin and Finite-Volume Method
eﬃcient implementation of the DG-FE method using the the local DG-FE op-
erators derived in the previous section. However, in order to easily identify the
model structure of the discretization, which will become necessary later when
implementing the linear and nonlinear Kalman ﬁlters, a global assembly based
on a set of global operators is proposed to discretize the EDM (2.4). The global
formulation is a convenient way of using the local operators to fully discretize
the nonlinear EDM on the form:
d
dtw(c(t)) = f(c(t), u(t)) = Acc(t) + Bcu(t),
(3.91a)
w(c(t)) = c(t) + Fq(c(t)),
(3.91b)
where the matrices Ac and Bc are the global discretization matrices. The vector
c(t) now contains the stacked values of the concentration for each element in each
discrete point, such that c(t) = (c1,1, c2,1, . . . cNp,1, c1,2, c2,2 . . . , cNp,Np×Ne)T and
u(t) = cin(t).
A shortcoming of the global formulation is, that due to the nature of the DG-FE
discretization, an extra state variable is needed to determine the ﬂuxes accu-
rately between each interface of the elements. This implies that cNp,i = c1,i+1
for i = 1, . . . , Np −1 when reﬁning the discretization. In principle, this leads
to redundancy in the global operators, which may be avoided by an implemen-
tation using only the local operators derived in Section 3.3.7.
However, the
accuracy of the DG-FE method ensures that relatively few states are necessary
to obtain an accurate numerical solution to (3.91). As such the trade-oﬀdoes
not represent an actual computational burden later during the implementation.
3.5.1
The Global DG-FE Operators
The global operators may be deﬁned in terms of discrete, fundamental ﬂux
operators E, F, G and H. These operators allow the surface integrals in (3.76)
to be given in terms of matrix based products of E, F, G and H. The action of
applying these operators, correspond to evaluating the Lagrange polynomials at
the end of each abscissas. These operators are given as:
Eij = ℓi(−1)ℓj(−1),
Fij = ℓi(−1)ℓj(1),
(3.92)
Gij = ℓi(1)ℓj(−1),
Hij = ℓi(1)ℓj(1).
(3.93)
The interpolating properties of the Lagrange polynomials ℓi(xj) = δij reduce
the discrete ﬂux operators to sparse matrices with ones in the separate corners.
==PAGE:63==
3.5 Global Assembly of the DG-FE and FV Method
49
They are the given in the following as:

1



1
,
(3.94)





3.5.2
The Global FV Operators
The following derives a ﬁrst-order, FV scheme, which is a standard solver used
for solving convection-diﬀusion equations. This method is derived to serve as a
benchmark in a comparative performance study with the DG-FE method.

When the equilibrium isotherm is nonlinear the continuous-time discrete-space
model is not given explicitly in terms of the concentration c. However, there are
several approaches one may consider to address this problem, and the following
section is dedicated to describing these approaches.
3.7
Solving the Nonlinear EDM
By spatial discretization of either the DG-FE or FV method the resulting system
admits the following form:
d
dtw(c(t)) = Acc(t) + Bcu(t),
(3.121a)
w(c(t)) = c(t) + Fq(c(t)).
(3.121b)
The discretized equations (3.121) may then be solved by a variety of approaches.
Speciﬁcally, three diﬀerent approaches are considered, and they are stated in the
following propositions:
==PAGE:69==
3.8 Temporal Discretization
55
Proposition 1. The chain rule is used to form a system of ODEs explicitly in
terms of the concentration c. The corresponding system given as:
d
dtc(t) = (I + FJ )−1 (Acc(t) + Bcu(t)) ,
(3.122)
is then solved by an adequate numerical time-step procedure to advance the solu-
tion in time, where I is the identity and J = ∂q
∂c is the Jacobian of the isotherm
relation q. The trade-oﬀof using the chain rule and forming the Jacobian, is
that the numerical scheme is non-conservative, which may lead to unphysical
solutions, where mass is lost. The proposition is still stated for completeness
however.
Proposition 2. The system (3.121) is solved by an adequate numerical time-
step procedure to advance the solution in time. This obtains the k + 1 solution
wk+1 = w(c(tk+1)). Since an explicit relationship for the function c(w) does
not exist for the nonlinear isotherm, the concentration ck+1 = c(tk+1) is instead
obtained by solving the equation:
w(c(tk+1)) = c(tk+1) + Fq(c(tk+1)),
(3.123)
Solutions to (3.123) may be computed by solving the residual equation R(c(tk+1)) =
w(c(tk+1)) −c(tk+1) −Fq(c(tk+1)) = 0 using Newton’s method.
Proposition 3. Begin by obtaining the solution w by solving (3.121).
The
concentration c is obtained by instead using the following implicit function for
c(w), which ensures a conservative formulation [DGM18]. The function is:
ci(w) =
wi
1 + Faiρ0(w),
(3.124)
where ρ0(w) is the only positive root of the following rational function:
Rw(y) = 1 −y +
Nc
X
i=1
y
y + Fai
biwi,
(3.125)
and 1 ≤ρ0(w) ≤1 + P
j bjwj, where the index refers to the individual com-
ponent of the multicomponent mixture.
The only positive root of (3.125) is
approximated using a nonlinear solver based on Kelley’s nsold algorithm, which
is Newton’s method using Newton-Armijo iterations, adaptive Jacobian updates
and parabolic line search via 3-point interpolation [Kel03]. The algorithm uses
a third party Matlab code nsold.m provided by [Kel03].
3.8
Temporal Discretization
The following section considers two approaches for propagating solutions to the
linear (3.118) and nonlinear (3.121) equations in time. A variety of methods
==PAGE:70==
56 Discretization by the Discontinuous-Galerkin and Finite-Volume Method
exist for advancing the solution in time, and whether the problem is linear
or nonlinear is signiﬁcant in determining a computationally attractive method.
The linear EDM leads to a discretized state-space model, which may integrated
exactly in time and solved by a series of matrix multiplications. The nonlinear
EDM requires more specialized iterative methods to propagate the solutions, and
to this end a low-storage, ﬁve-stage fourth-order explicit, Runge-Kutta method
(LSERK) is proposed as a computationally accurate time-stepper.
3.8.1
Numerical Temporal Discretization
In order to discretize the general, semi-discrete problem in time:
d
dtw(c) = L(c, t),
(3.126)
where L is some right-hand-side function, that appears from the spatial dis-
cretization, one can use the classical fourth-order, four-stage explicit Runge-
Kutta method (ERK). The method advances the solution in time by the time
step ∆t from cn to cn+1. The method is given by the following procedure:
k(1) = L(cn, tn),
(3.127a)
k(2) = L

cn + 1
2∆tk(1), tn + 1
2∆t

,
(3.127b)
k(3) = L

cn + 1
2∆tk(2), tn + 1
2∆t

,
(3.127c)
k(4) = L(cn + ∆tk(3), tn + ∆t),
(3.127d)
w(cn+1) = cn + 1
6

k(1) + 2k(2) + 2k(3) + k(4)
.
(3.127e)
The method in (3.127) is a widely adopted method, but it has the downside
of being memory expensive, since it requires four extra storage arrays, k(i),
i = 1, . . . , 4. An attractive alternative to this method is the low-storage version
(LSERK) [KC94, HW08] of the form:
p(0) = c(tn)
(3.128a)
i ∈[1, . . . , 5] :
(
k(i) = aik(i−1) + ∆tf(p(i−1), tn + ci∆t),
p(i) = p(i−1) + bik(i),
(3.128b)
w(cn+1) = p(5).
(3.128c)

diﬀers from ERK method, since it uses an additional storage level. However,

3.10
Numerical Study
This section investigates three numerical case studies of the DG-FE and FV
method: 1) the ﬁrst case study veriﬁes the h-p adaptivity of the DG-FE method
and ﬁrst order convergence of the FV method. 2) The second study investigates
a performance comparison between the two solvers, in order to verify the su-
perior performance of the high-order method. 3) The third case study demon-
strates the capabilities of the high-order method for solving coupled, nonlinear
problems. To this end, a well established nonlinear scenario describing a binary
mixture is investigated [MG92, MHA18b].
3.10.1
Numerical Case I: Convergence of the DG-FE and
FV Method
The ﬁrst case study investigates simulation of a chromatographic process with
one component and a linear equilibrium isotherm described by the linear EDM
(2.29). The column parameters used in the linear case study are listed in Table
3.2, and resemble a realistic case study by [QAJ+13]. The feed concentration
cf is injected over a time total period of tinj = 2 min. The total simulation time
spans a period of tf = 5 min, and the sampling time is set to Ts = 0.01 min.
The linear EDM is solved by discretizing the spatial ﬂux according to (3.131)
by using the global DG-FE operators ˜Ac and ˜Bc. The temporal variable is dis-
cretized using an exact discretization, resulting in a system on the form (3.132).
The solution was found by propagating (3.132) using a polynomial order N = 20
and Ne = 30 elements.
Figure 3.7 shows a collection of snapshots of the solution to the linear EDM
(2.29) for increasing time steps using the aforementioned column parameters
and simulation scenario. The concentration band propagates left through right
in the chromatographic column. During this propagation band broadening of

As previously described in Section 2.5.1, the analytical solution is known for
the linear EDM [van81, QAJ+13]. The availability of this solution allows for an
evaluation of the error using the exact ℓ2(Ω) norm in the spatial domain, and
as a way to address the adaptivity of the DG-FE and FV method. The discrete
==PAGE:76==
62 Discretization by the Discontinuous-Galerkin and Finite-Volume Method
ℓ2(Ω)-norm over the domain Ω= (0, L) is:
||ea||ℓ2(Ω) = ||c −ca||ℓ2(Ω),
(3.134a)
=

(c −ca)2
! 1
2
.
(3.134b)
The DOFs, Nz, of each method is either the number quadrature points times
number of elements Nz = Np × Ne for the DG-FE method or the total number
of cells for the FV method. The error, ea, is measured as the diﬀerence:
ea = c −ca,
(3.135)
between the approximate DG-FE or FV solution (denoted c) and the known
analytical solution (denoted ca) given in (2.30).
In the following consider again the column parameters listed in Table 3.2. The
feed concentration cf is injected over a time total period of tinj = 2 min, and
consider a ﬁnal simulation time of tf = 2 min.
The solution to the linear
EDM is simulated using both the DG-FE and FV method for increasing DOFs.
After each successful simulation the error between the analytical solution and
approximate solution is computed in the right most grid point, using the discrete
ℓ2-norm (3.134b).
Figure 3.9a demonstrates the h-p adaptivity of the DG-FE method. The h-
reﬁnement is demonstrated for N ∈{1, 2, 3, 4, 5} for increasing number of el-
ements, and the p- reﬁnement by ﬁxing the number of elements Ne = 10 and
increasing the polynomial order N.
This is due to the spectral convergence
property, which is tied to the smoothness of the solution explained previously
[HW08]. A tolerance of ϵ = 10−10 is achieved after only 300 DOFs using p-
reﬁnement of the DG-FE method.
Figure 3.9b demonstrates the ﬁrst-order convergence of the FV method when
increasing the number of ﬁnite-volume cells Nz ∈{200, 400, . . . , 2000}.
The
error of the FV method decays at rate O(∆z) when increasing the reﬁnement
∆z, since the method is ﬁrst-order accurate. As a result the FV method requires
many more DOFs than the DG-FE method to reach a desirable accuracy. In
terms of accuracy, the DG-FE method is able to compute reliable solutions,
by an order of magnitude faster than the FV method, and therefore represents
a competitive alternative to conventional high-performance methods for fast
chromatographic simulations.

3.10.2
Numerical Case II: Performance Comparison of the
DG-FE and FV Method
The second case study compares the computational performance of the DG-
FE and FV methods ability to reach a desired tolerance, ϵ, by increasing the
precision of both numerical schemes by increasing the DOFs.
Figure 3.10 shows such a comparison between the DG-FE and FV method.
Each dot in the Figure represents a single simulation, and is characterized by
the accuracy and DOFs of each particular simulation. The parameters used for
simulating are similar to the previously used ones, and they are listed in Table
3.2.
The ﬁgure also compares the required CPU-time necessary to compute
the solution by (3.132) for each method. Increasing the DOFs requires more
computational time for both the DG-FE and FV method. However, due to the
h-p adaptivity, the DG-FE method is able to mitigate this performance burden,
since the method requires far less states to reach a desirable accuracy. As a
consequence, the DG-FE method stands out as a much faster alternative to the
FV method, and is in the following solely considered from now on, as the desired
numerical simulator.

Remark 2. Note, that for increasing number of DOFs, the matrix exponential
in (3.133) becomes increasingly time burdening to compute. The FV method
requires many states to reach high accuracy, and therefore the additional cost
of computing the matrix exponential becomes quite signiﬁcant. However, this is
not included in the timing, since the matrix exponential is considered an oﬄine
computation.
3.10.3
Numerical Case III: DG-FE Method for
Multi-Component Nonlinear Modeling
The third case study considers simulation of a binary mixture chromatographic
process, with a nonlinear equilibrium relation modeled by the competitive Lang-
muir isotherm (2.27). Since the model is nonlinear and has two components, the
dynamics are described by a system of two coupled PDEs in (2.4). The column
parameters for the two-component system is given in Table 3.3. The column
parameters represent a realistic benchmark study by [MG92, JQA+13, SSH08].
The problem is simulated under liquid chromatography conditions with Nt =
500 theoretical plates and under HPLC conditions with Nt = 5000 theoretical
plates. The feed concentration cf,1 and cf,2 of both components are injected
over a time total period of tinj = 12 min. The ﬁnal simulation time is set to
tf = 40 min. The CFL condition is set to C = 0.3 for numerically stable solu-
tion. The solution is simulated under liquid chromatography conditions using
a polynomial order N = 5 and Ne = 100 elements, using the technique de-
scribed in Proposition 3. In general, the technique described in Proposition
3 proved to be almost twice as fast as Proposition 2.
To demonstrate the capabilities of the DG-FE method the dynamics are also
simulated under HPLC conditions. The system is simulated using N = 5 order
polynomials and Ne = 1000 elements. For columns with higher eﬃciencies, that
is larger number of theoretical plates, the DOFs necessary for computing stable
solutions increase as well, due to the nature of the CFL condition in (3.129).
Figure 3.11 shows the concentrations of each component at the inlet and outlet
of the chromatographic column under liquid chromatographic conditions and
under HPLC conditions. The dynamics illustrated in the left window of Figure
3.11, show the formation of a shock layer proﬁle. The simulation demonstrates,
that strong nonlinearities produce overshoots in the concentration proﬁle, which
is caused by the choice of nonlinear isotherm. This is a well-known phenomena
in chromatography [MHA18b]. Furthermore, for ﬁnite dispersion coeﬃcients,
the shock layer at the band front has a thickness greater than zero [MG92].
Therefore, the shock layer proﬁle does not represent a true discontinuity, which

nearly vertical as a result of the high eﬃciency of the column. Even though the

still allow for an accurate approximation of the shock proﬁles of the solution.
==PAGE:81==
C h a p t e r 4
State Estimation of
Chromatographic Processes
State estimation is a technique, which is used to reconstruct a state variable of
a given process. The reconstruction is based on: 1) noisy measurements and
2) a dynamical model of the process. The estimator therefore acts as a medi-
ator, and provides a most likely estimate of the state given the physical model
and measurement information. Figure 1.4 in Chapter 1 provides a conceptual
illustration of this principle.
The following chapter considers state estimation of chromatographic processes.
These processes are modeled by stochastic, continuous-discrete 1 systems, which
are on the form:
dg(x(t)) =
Deterministic
z
}|
{
f(t, x(t), u(t))dt +
Stochastic
z
}|
{
σ(t)dω(t),
(4.1a)
g(x(t)) = x(t) + Fq(x(t)),
(4.1b)
y(tk) = h(tk, x(tk)) + vk.
(4.1c)
The compact model in (4.1) represents a semi-discrete system, which consists
of a deterministic and stochastic term. The deterministic term appears when
1The continuous-discrete formulation implies that the modeled dynamics are continuous
and the measurements are discrete.
==PAGE:82==
68
State Estimation of Chromatographic Processes
the spatial derivatives of the EDM are discretized using the DG-FE method. In
addition the model is augmented with a stochastic Brownian motion σ(t)dω(t),
which models the uncertainty of the process. Details of this model are provided
in the subsequent sections.
The following provides descriptions of diﬀerent state estimation techniques for
the system of equations in (4.1) when the dynamics of the EDM (2.4) are lin-
ear and nonlinear. These models require diﬀerent state estimation techniques,
therefore this chapter naturally divides into two separate parts: 1) the ﬁrst part
considers a stochastic state-space model, which is derived when the model in
(4.1) is linear, that is when the equilibrium relation is linear q(x(t)) = ax(t).
The Kalman ﬁlter is used as an optimal linear estimator for this problem. 2)
The second part consider the nonlinear stochastic, continuous-discrete model
in (4.1), that is when the equilibrium relation is nonlinear q(x(t)) = f(x(t)).
To this end four ﬁlters: the EKF, UKF, PF and the EnKF are developed for
state estimation, when the dynamics of the EDM are described by the Langmuir
isotherm (2.27).
The state estimation techniques considered in this thesis all consist of two parts.
Whether the model is linear or nonlinear, these parts are: 1) a measurement-
update and 2) a time-update to estimate the uncertain states. In the measurement-
update the information from the discrete measurement is used to estimate the
current state.
This estimate uses information from the physical model (4.1)
and the measurement device (sensor). In the time-update the dynamics of the
EDM is used to advance the state estimate in time between each discrete mea-
surement. Figure 4.1 provides a conceptual illustration of this principle. The
time-update is typically the computational bottleneck of the nonlinear ﬁlters
[Jør07, RJ18, Sim06], as it requires solution of many nonlinear ODEs, in order
to advance the state and covariance estimates. The linear Kalman ﬁlter is not
as computationally demanding, as the update is computed by simply advancing
the state-space model, which is less computationally demanding, using either
an oﬄine (stationary ﬁlter) or online (dynamic ﬁlter) approach.
Measurement-Update
Time-Update
Measurement-Update
Time-Update

Figure 4.1: Conceptual illustration of the measurement- and time-update in
the Kalman ﬁlters.
To keep with the notation of relevant literature [Jør07, JTMK07, RJ18], note
that x(t) is now the state vector of concentrations, u(t) is the inlet concentration
==PAGE:83==
4.1 Linear State Estimation
69
cin,i(t) in (2.9), f is the right hand side resulting from the spatial discretization
and the relations (2.5) and (4.1b) are equivalent by a scaling with the porosity
ϵ.
In presence of an unmeasured disturbance, a disturbance model is necessary
to guarantee oﬀset-free control of the ﬁltered state. The inlet concentration of
the chromatographic column is typically not known for industrial feed stocks,
and may be modeled as an unknown disturbance. The unknown disturbance,
xd(t), is assumed to evolve as stochastic Brownian motion dxd(t) = σddωd(t),
and is augmented as an extra state in the model (4.1). The augmented model
may then be used to estimate the states and unknown input simultaneously.
A measurement equation for each augmented disturbance state is necessary to
guarantee a valid disturbance model [PR03].
Finally, the main purpose of this chapter is to provide a systematic description
of each state estimation technique used in this thesis. However, the purpose
is not to provide derivations of each ﬁlter, but rather to provide an algorith-
mic understanding of each ﬁlter. The following sections consider and provide
equations for 1) the Kalman ﬁlter and 2) the nonlinear ﬁlters.
4.1
Linear State Estimation
The following section introduces the stochastic state-space model.
Consider
the model in (4.1).
Given a linear isotherm, the deterministic part may be
discretized according to the procedure in Section 3.8.2 using a ZOH parame-
terization of the time and input. This model assumes that the noise is kept
constant over each discrete time interval tk. As a consequence the process noise
is modeled as a piecewise stochastic noise term wk.
The dynamic and stationary Kalman ﬁlters are presented for state estimation
of the state-space model. Given such a model, the Kalman ﬁlter is the best
linear estimator. Given the stationary Kalman ﬁlter, several of the matrices are
considered static and may be precomputed [Sim06].
==PAGE:84==
70
State Estimation of Chromatographic Processes
4.1.1
Stochastic Linear State-Space Model
The stochastic state-space model is a realization of the discretized EDM, and
consists of a deterministic and stochastic part. This model is on the form:
xk+1 = Axk + Buk + Gwk,
(4.2a)
zk = Cxk,
(4.2b)
yk = zk + vk,
(4.2c)
and is a simpliﬁed representation of the model in (4.1) based on the previous
assumptions. This corresponds to conversion from a continuous-time represen-
tation to an equivalent discrete-time representation. The initial state x0, process
noise wk and measurement noise vk are identically ideally distributed as:
x0 ∼N(¯x0, P0),
(4.3a)
wk ∼Niid(0, Q),
(4.3b)
vk ∼Niid(0, R).
(4.3c)
Note that N(µ, Σ) is a normal distribution with mean µ and covariance Σ.
4.1.2
Dynamic Kalman Filter
Assume that the mean and covariance of the initial state (4.3a) are known. The
dynamic Kalman ﬁlter equations may then be stated as follows. The one-step
ahead prediction of the state, ˆxk|k−1, and its covariance, Pk|k−1, are computed
as:
ˆxk|k−1 = Aˆxk−1|k−1 + Buk−1,
(4.4a)
Pk|k−1 = APk−1|k−1AT + GQGT .
(4.4b)
Using the measurement equation in (4.2), the one-step ahead prediction of the
measurement, ˆyk|k−1, may be computed as:
ˆyk|k−1 = Cˆxk|k−1,
(4.5)
The error between the measurement and physical model, which is known as the
innovation ek, is obtained by:
ek = yk −ˆyk|k−1,
(4.6a)
Re,k = CPk|k−1CT + R,
(4.6b)

Kfx,k = Pk|k−1CT R−1
e,k.
(4.7)

(4.8a)
Pk|k = Pk|k−1 −Kfx,kRe,kKT

semideﬁnite.
The Kalman ﬁlter is summarized in the following algorithm.

4.1.3
Stationary Kalman Filter
The stationary Kalman ﬁlter is a computationally more attractive alternative
to the dynamic ﬁlter, since the matrices may be precomputed oﬄine.
The
stationary Kalman ﬁlter equations may be stated as follows. The equations for
computing the ﬁltered state are equivalent to the ones presented for the dynamic
Kalman ﬁlter. However, the covariance is no longer time-dependent and may
be precomputed as P.
The covariance, P, is obtained by solving the Discrete-Algebraic-Ricatti-Equation
(DARE):
P = APAT + GQGT −(APCT )(CPCT + R)−1(APCT )T ,
(4.10)
which is used to compute the stationary state and the ﬁlter gains:
Re = CPCT + R,
(4.11a)
Kfx = PCT R−1

4.1.4
Linear Disturbance Modeling
In order to estimate the unknown inlet concentrations of the chromatographic
column, the model in (4.2) requires a modiﬁcation. A disturbance model may be
used to estimate the ﬁltered states and uncertain concentrations simultaneously.

4.2
MATLAB Implementation II
In the following, brief comments on the MATLAB implementation of the Kalman
ﬁlter for the linear EDM are given. Figure 3.6 illustrates the workﬂow in the
practical MATLAB implementation of the nonlinear solver. The example given
in Figure 4.2 assumes a linear system with one component, and estimates the
uncertain states and inlet concentration simultaneously. The MATLAB code
uses the dynamic Kalman ﬁlter equations in Section 4.1.2, and is implemented
based on Algorithm 1. The MATLAB code also features an implementation of
the stationary ﬁlter, using the static equations described in Section 4.1.3. The
operations performed in the dark grey colored box are oﬄine, and the estimation
performed in the light grey box are online.
Linear_Matrices_DGFE.m
   -  Defines the discretized global flux

State Estimation of Chromatographic Processes
4.3
Nonlinear State Estimation
The following section introduces state estimation for the nonlinear EDM. Con-
sider the following stochastic, continuous-discrete nonlinear system, i.e. the sys-
tem with continuous dynamics and discrete measurements formulated in (4.1).
For completeness the model is again stated as the following:
dg(x(t)) = f(t, x(t), u(t))dt + σ(t)dω(t),
(4.20a)
g(x(t)) = x(t) + Fq(x(t)),
(4.20b)
y(tk) = h(tk, x(tk)) + vk,
(4.20c)
where {ω(t), t ≥0} is a standard Weiner process, i.e. a Wiener process with
incremental covariance Idt, and the measurement noise is normally distributed
vk = v(tk) ∼Nidd(0, Rk).
The dynamics of (4.20) are continuous and the
measurements y(tk) are obtained at discrete times tk. Assume knowledge of the
initial concentration (state) in the chromatographic column x(t0) ∼N(x0, P0).
This initial estimate has a degree of uncertainty, which is modeled by the initial
covariance P0. The structure in (3.108) identiﬁes the right hand side f of the
continuous-discrete dynamics as f = Acx(t) + Bcu(t). This corresponds to a
system on the form:
dg(x(t)) = (Acx(t) + Bcu(t))dt + σ(t)dω(t),
(4.21a)
g(x(t)) = x(t) + Fq(x(t)),
(4.21b)
y(tk) = Cx(tk) + vk.
(4.21c)
Note that parallel to the stochastic state-space model in (4.17), the measure-
ments are still taken at the outlet, so the matrix C in the measurement equation
(4.21c) remains linear as C = [0, 0, . . . , 1].
In the following, consider four diﬀerent algorithms for state estimation of the
continuous-discrete equations describing the nonlinear chromatographic process
in (4.21c). The ﬁlters considered are the:
1) Extended Kalman Filter (EKF).
2) Ensemble Kalman Filter (EnKF).
3) Particle Filter (PKF).
4) Unscented Kalman Filter (UKF).
The following sections introduces the governing equations describing each ﬁlter,
and provides measurement and time-updates for the continuous-discrete model.
==PAGE:91==
4.3 Nonlinear State Estimation
77
4.3.1
The Extended Kalman Filter
This section describes the EKF for the continuous-discrete model (4.21). The
EKF applies the original Kalman ﬁlter equations in Section 4.1.2 to a lineariza-
tion of the nonlinear model equations in (4.21) [Jør07, JTMK07, Sim06, RJ18].
Assume that the EKF is initialized with mean-covariance pair of the initial
states given as:
ˆx0|−1 = x0,
(4.22)
P0|−1 = P0.
(4.23)
The continuous-discrete EKF for ﬁltering and prediction then consists of a mea-
surement and time-update. These updates are stated as follows.
Measurement-update
The one-step ahead prediction of the measurement, ˆyk|k−1 = yk−1(tk), and its

ˆyk|k−1 = h(tk, ˆxk|k−1),

(4.27)
The Kalman ﬁlter gain matrix, Kfx,k, is computed as:
Kfx,k = Pk|k−1CT

k|k−1.

measurement update.

Time-update
The above formulas are for the measurement-update, and they are equivalent to
the discrete time case. The diﬀerence between the discrete-time and continuous-
time extended Kalman Filter arises in the one-step ahead propagation of the
state estimate and its covariance.
The major computational operation in the continuous-discrete extended Kalman
ﬁlter is the integration of the mean-covariance pair ˆxk+1|k = ˆxk(tk+1) and
Pk+1|k = Pk(tk+1), which advances the states and covariance from the present
time-step to the next. The one-step ahead propagation of the mean-covariance
pair is computed as the solution to the system of nonlinear ODEs:

concentration u(t). This choice leads to deﬁning the diﬀusion term σ(t) in (4.21)
as σ(t) = Bc.

4.3.2

4.3.3
The Particle Kalman Filter
This section describes the PF for the continuous-discrete model (4.21). The
PF is a particle ﬁlter, which uses a recursive likelihood update, based on a set
of particles to capture the probability distribution of the uncertain states. The
particle size of the PF is, like the ensemble size of the EnKF, a tuning parameter
of the ﬁlter. In other words, the PF and EnKF are almost identical, and only
the measurement-update between of each ﬁlter is diﬀerent.
In general, the EnKF and PF belong to a broader class of methods known as
Monte-Carlo methods. In principle, these methods may be used to solve any
given problem, which has a probabilistic interpretation. Since, by the law of
large numbers, expected values of random variables can be approximated by
computing the mean over some set of independent samples of that variable.
This is also the fundamental idea behind the PF and EnKF.
Following the approach of [Sim06, RJ18], assume that the PF is initialized with
==PAGE:97==
4.3 Nonlinear State Estimation

4.3.5

In order to estimate the unknown inlet concentration, the continuous-discrete
model (4.20) is augmented with a disturbance variable:
dxd(t) = σddωd(t).
(4.4)

described in Section 4.1.4, and by augmenting with the disturbance state (4.4),
the augmented model takes the form:
