the sensor, h, is still h = [C, Cd] for the nonlinear disturbance model.
4.3.6
Discussion of the Nonlinear State Estimation Algo-
rithms
The following covers a brief discussion of the four diﬀerent ﬁlters: EKF, UKF,
PF and EnKF.
==PAGE:103==
4.3 Nonlinear State Estimation
89
The EKF is probably among the most widely adopted nonlinear state estimation
technologies for nonlinear systems [Jør07]. The EKF linearizes the nonlinear
model and applies the original Kalman ﬁlter equations, as previously described.
This is a ﬁrst-order approximation, and may lead the EKF to be imprecise for
highly nonlinear system. Furthermore, continuous-time discrete-measurement
systems such as (4.20) require integration of the mean-covariance pair. This
integration may for large-scale systems become intractable, since it requires
the solution to n2
x (where nx is the number of states) nonlinear ODEs at each
iteration, and an increase in the number of states, results in an increase in the
number of nonlinear ODEs, which needs to be solved during each time-update.
Finally, the EKF requires the evaluation of the Jacobian. For some systems the
Jacobian may be diﬃcult to evaluate, but this is not much of a bottleneck, since
most algorithms require the Jacobian to run eﬃciently anyway.
The UKF uses a deterministic set of samples to improve the accuracy of the
states estimates and generally produces more accurate estimates than the EKF
[VDMAWIJ09, RJ18, Sim06]. This improved accuracy comes at a price, and
requires 2˜n + 1 simulations where ˜n = nx + nω and two Cholesky factorizations
of the covariance matrix at each iteration. For large-scale systems with many
states, the number of simulations and factorizations may become computation-
ally demanding as well.
The PF and EnKF are particle based (Monte-Carlo) approaches that use a set
of random samples to recover the distribution of the states either by likelihood
estimation (PF) or a recursive least squares update (EnKF). There is no exact
number of particles/ensembles that guarantee an accurate state estimate, and
this question remains an open research topic [GBMC+09]. This is opposed to
the UKF, which uses a deterministic set of samples. However, this is both a
strength and weakness of the particle ﬁlters, as a fewer set of ensembles (and
computational workload) may be needed to accurately capture the distribution
of state estimates compared to the EKF and UKF. However, the ability to cap-
ture the distribution of the ﬁltered states, similarly seems to be highly problem
and parameter dependent. As an example [GBMC+09] suggests in between 50-
100 ensembles, while the amount of particles needed may be in the number of
10000s as reported by [TGK16]. However, the PF/EnKF would be expected to
require less computational time for large-scale systems, as no factorization or
covariance integration is required in the PF and EnKF, which becomes a serious
bottleneck for the EKF and UKF for large enough systems.
However, many of the computations during each update of the sample based
methods: UKF, PF and EnKF are computed independent of each other and
introduce the possibility of being computed in parallel. To this end, the time
spent during integration of the mean (and integration of the covariance matrix
in the EKF) in the time-updates may be heavily brought down. Perhaps this
==PAGE:104==
90
State Estimation of Chromatographic Processes
makes the probabilistic approaches more computationally feasible compared to
the EKF.

In the following, brief comments on the MATLAB implementation regarding
the four nonlinear ﬁlters are given. Figure 4.6 illustrates the workﬂow in the
practical MATLAB implementation of the four nonlinear ﬁlters. The workﬂow
illustrated in Figure 4.6 assumes a system with one component is modeled by the
nonlinear EDM (2.4), and estimates the uncertain states and inlet concentration
simultaneously. The MATLAB code uses each of the four nonlinear ﬁlters. The
operations performed in the dark grey colored box are considered oﬄine, while
the estimation in the light grey box is considered online. The scripts have been
setup to run on the DTU HPC cluster 3. This was done in order to compute
the PF and EnKF for varying sample sizes and save time.

4.5
Numerical Study
This section investigates ﬁve numerical case studies of the linear and nonlinear
state estimation problem: 1) the ﬁrst case study investigates state estimation of
the linear EDM (2.29) using the Kalman ﬁlter. To demonstrate the capability
of using high-order methods, the ﬁrst study compares the performance of the
DG-FE and FV method for computing the ﬁltered states in terms of accuracy,
DOFs and simulation time. 2) The second study demonstrates the use of the
augmented state-space model in (4.19) to estimate the unknown inlet concentra-
tion under varying inﬂuence of the disturbance tuning. 3) The third case study
investigates state estimation of the nonlinear EDM (2.4) using the four nonlin-
ear ﬁlters: EKF, UKF, PF and EnKF. A comparison between each algorithm is

4.5.1
Numerical Case I: Comparison of the DG-FE and
FV Method for Filtering
The column parameters are identical to the linear case study in Section 3.10.2,
and for completeness they are listed again in Table 4.1. Note that the initial
concentration, c0, has been modiﬁed and is now c0 = 2. This choice is made to
avoid unphysical, negative concentrations in the estimation. The feed concen-
tration cf is injected over a time period of tinj = 2 min. The ﬁnal simulation
time is tf = 2 min. The sampling time is Ts = 0.1 min. The measurements
arrive at intervals of 6 seconds. The noise covariances used in the simulation are
Q = R = 0.12, and the disturbance model uses a tuning parameter Qξ = 0.0012.
The initial covariance is the unity P0 = I. The DG-FE simulation uses Ne = 30
elements and polynomials of order N ∈{2, 3, . . . , 14}, and the FV method
uses Nz ∈{800, 1000, 1200, . . . , 4000} DOFs (ﬁnite-volume cells). The dynamic
Kalman ﬁlter described in Section 4.1.2 is used as the optimal estimator.
Figure 4.7 compares the performance of the DG-FE and FV method, when es-
timating the inlet concentration using the disturbance model in (4.19). The
ﬁgure compares each methods capability for estimating the uncertain states in
the disturbance model in terms of the required CPU-time and DOFs. The toler-
ance, ϵ, corresponds to the accuracy of the numerical method used to compute
the ﬁltered states.
The results are parallel to the comparison and conclusion made in Figure 3.10,
and as expected the DG-FE method is signiﬁcantly faster by an order of magni-

4.5.2
Numerical Case II: Linear Model Input Estimation
Figure 4.8 compares three diﬀerent realizations of the estimated inlet concen-

and the sampling time is Ts = 0.1 min resulting in k = 160 time steps. The
measurements again arrive at intervals of 6 seconds. The DG-FE simulation in
Figure 4.8 uses Ne = 30 elements and polynomial order N = 10. The dynamic
Kalman ﬁlter is used with an initial covariance matrix P0 = I. The process and
measurement noise covariances are Q = 0.12 and R = 0.22. Figure 4.8 considers

==PAGE:108==

The input is estimated after a natural time delay of approximately 3 minutes,
since the measurements only begin to appear at the outlet of the column after
the process has undergone a certain amount of time.
For low values of the
tuning parameter, the inlet concentration is estimated as a smooth transition.
Increasing the value of the tuning parameter allows the estimate to capture more
of the sharp proﬁle of the inlet concentration. However, this is compromised by
a more aggressive estimate, as seen in Figure 4.8, and naturally a trade-oﬀin
this transition occurs.
The placement of the sensor at the column outlet represents a worst-case sce-
nario, where measurements only become available at the outlet. However, if the
sensor is placed farther towards the inlet of the chromatographic column, the
time delay in the estimation would naturally be mitigated. Figure 4.9 shows
three diﬀerent realizations of the estimated inputs for diﬀerent placements of
the sensor at the inlet, middle and outlet of the column. As anticipated the
inlet concentration is estimated with a decreasing time delay, when moving the
sensor towards the inlet. The placement of the sensor at the outlet represents
the most realistic scenario, as measurements of industrial feed stocks typically
only appear at the outlet. In order to mitigate the delay in the estimated inlet
concentration, a possible solution could be to incorporate a time delay into the
numerical model, by knowledge of how fast the band proﬁle propagates towards

Figure 4.10, Table 4.3 and Table 4.4 compare a set of simulations of the EKF,
UKF, PF and EnKF for varying particle and ensemble sizes. Each dot in Figure
4.10 represents a simulation by either of the four ﬁlters. The PF and EnKF are
tuned by the number of particles/ensembles, which produces a trade-oﬀbetween
the RMSD and required CPU-time.
Figure 4.10 compares the RMSD with the total CPU-time for one measurement
and time-update. Table 4.3 and Table 4.4 shows, the individual time-cost for
one measurement- and one time-update for each of the four ﬁlters. The variable
CPU1 corresponds to the average CPU-time spent during one measurements
update in milliseconds (ms). The variable CPU2 corresponds to the average
CPU-time spent during one time-update in milliseconds (ms). The RMSD is
calculated between the process simulation, xk, and the estimated states, ˆxk|k,
at the outlet of the column at z = 1 cm.
Figure 4.10 shows that the EKF updates the estimated states signiﬁcantly faster
than both the UKF, PF and EnKF, and that the EKF is able to produce rea-
sonably accurate state estimates, which in terms of the RMSD lie close to the
process solution. For the same accuracy, the UKF performs signiﬁcantly worse
as the average CPU-time is much higher in comparison to the EKF. The PF re-
quires many particles and signiﬁcant amounts of CPU-time to produce accurate
state estimates compared to the other ﬁlters. For low computational cost how-
ever, the EnKF is able to produce reasonably accurate state estimates as well,
but consequently also requires signiﬁcantly more computational time compared
to the EKF.

Figure 4.10: Comparison of the average CPU-time (seconds) for one mea-
surement + time-update of the EKF, UKF, PF and EnKF
for diﬀerent ensemble sizes.
The PF considers particle sizes
q ∈{10, 30, 80, 160, 180, 200} and the EnKF considers ensemble
sizes q ∈{2, 6, 10, 30, 80, 160, 180, 200} .
As anticipated from the results in Table 4.3 and 4.4 the main computational
bottleneck of each ﬁlter is the propagation of the mean state and covariance in
the time-update. The EKF propagates the mean-covariance pair, by solving n2
x
sensitivity ODEs and nx mean state ODEs. The UKF requires solution of nx
mean state ODEs for each of the 2nx + 1 samples. The number of states for the
discretization used in each simulation is nx = Np × Ne = 66.
Recall, that during each time-update the mean state is propagated by solving
nx ODEs, and during each iteration of the LSERK method, a Newton approx-
imation is performed to recover the concentration c.
Since the UKF uses a
deterministic set of samples, this creates a much larger computational burden
for the ﬁlter, since the discretization of the PDE naturally lead to a larger num-
ber of states, and in turn more Newton approximations need to be computed.
The PF and EnKF require the solution of nx mean state ODEs for each parti-
cle/ensemble during the time-update. However, the sample size is tunable and
is not ﬁxed as the UKF, so the computational burden is not as large for the
lower sample sizes.
The results of this study highlight two points: 1) the EKF is signiﬁcantly faster
than the other ﬁlters and able to produce reasonably accurate state estimates.

and may easily be parallelized, which would lower the computational cost signif-
icantly without compromising accuracy. 2) The conclusions made in this study
does not necessarily reﬂect the overall performance of the ﬁlters as their perfor-
mance is highly problem and parameter dependent [RJ18, GBMC+09, TGK16].

ensembles.

shown in Section 4.5.2. However, the main diﬀerence is now that the EDM is a
nonlinear model. In Figure 4.12 measurements appear every 5 seconds, and both
ﬁlters are able to estimate the inlet concentration after a delay of approximately
3 min. In Figure 4.13 the measurements appear every 9 seconds instead. The ﬁl-
ters remain capable of estimating the uncertain inlet concentration, even though
fewer measurements are provided to the EKF and EnKF. However, the sparsity
of the data provides state estimates, which are wrought with a higher degree
of uncertainty, and the estimates ﬂuctuate around the true inlet concentration

4.5.5
Numerical Case V: Nonlinear Estimation of a Binary
Mixture
The ﬁnal case study considers considers state estimation of the outlet concen-
tration of a binary mixture. The parameters are identical to the nonlinear case
study in Section 3.10.3 and are listed in Table 4.5. The problem is simulated
under liquid chromatography conditions with Nt = 250 theoretical plates. Dis-
persion in the outlet proﬁles is therefore more apparent in this example. The
feed concentration cf,1 and cf,2 of both components are injected over a time to-
tal period of tinj = 12 min. The ﬁnal simulation time is set to tf = 40 min. The
CFL condition is set to C = 0.3 for numerically stable solution. The solution
under liquid chromatography conditions is simulated using a polynomial order
N = 5 and Ne = 12 elements. Measurements of both components arrive every
4 seconds. The noise covariances are Q = ∆t = 0.069 and R = 0.82. The initial
state of both components follow a normal distribution ˆx0|−1 ∼N(c0, P0), where
P0 = 0.1 is the initial covariance of both components.

work on this project. There are many other suggestions for future work, and
these suggestions are outlined in the ﬁnal chapter along with conclusions of the
work presented in this thesis.
==PAGE:117==
C h a p t e r 5
Outlook
5.1
Conclusion
The primary goals of this thesis were tied to the development and implemen-
tation of: 1) a high-order numerical method to accurately model the governing
linear and nonlinear, coupled convection-dominated PDEs, that constitutes the
equilibrium dispersive model of chromatography. 2) Linear and nonlinear state
estimation algorithms for state estimation of chromatographic processes.
Based on the work in this thesis, the following overall conclusions can be made:
• Compared to a conventional FV method, the DG-FE method was able to
produce highly accurate solutions to the EDM. These solutions used signif-
icantly fewer DOFs, and were computed faster by an order of magnitude.
As a consequence, the DG-FE method was able to produce solutions to
the EDM much faster than the FV method. The DG-FE method was able
to simulate single and multi-component ﬂows inside the chromatographic
column.
Speciﬁcally, the method was capable of accurately simulating
well-established nonlinear cases and capturing nearly vertical shock pro-
ﬁles, that forms when simulating the model under HPLC conditions.
==PAGE:118==
104
Outlook
• The state-space model developed for the linear EDM was used in con-
junction with the dynamic Kalman to estimate the uncertain inlet con-
centration of the process, by using an augmented model formulation. The
DG-FE method proved to be advantageous to the FV method in terms of
accuracy and eﬃciency, when using the Kalman ﬁlter as state estimator.
Speciﬁcally, the DG-FE method was able to produce state estimates much
faster, using signiﬁcantly less states and with much higher model accuracy
when compared to the FV method.
• Algorithms for the EKF, UKF, PF and EnKF were developed and imple-
mented, for estimation and comparison of the uncertain outlet concentra-
tions, of the chromatographic process described by the nonlinear EDM.
The EKF proved to be the most computationally feasible among these
four nonlinear ﬁlters, and was able to produce reasonably accurate state
estimates. A deterministic and probabilistic approach towards nonlinear
input estimation was given by a comparison between the EKF and EnKF.
Both ﬁlters were capable of estimating the uncertain inlet concentrations
using an augmented model formulation of the nonlinear model. Finally,
the EKF was capable of estimating the outlet concentrations of a chro-
matographic process featuring a binary mixture.
In conclusion, the goals put forward have been matched with expectation and
promising results. The DG-FE method shows great potential as a high-ﬁdelity
solver for chromatographic processes.
The capability of the DG-FE method
for simulating these processes using few states is crucial.
This capability is
necessary for the nonlinear state estimation algorithms as well. Since the number
of states ultimately depends on the reﬁnement of the discretization, and the state
estimation algorithms need to produce reliable estimates in the shortest amount
of time, if they are to be used in online estimation in NMPC.
5.2
Recommendations for Future Work
This section presents suggestions for future work prospects, that build and ex-
tend upon the work in this project. To this end, the following recommendations
are given:
• The work in this thesis considers the EDM, which is the least complex
of the three models described in Section 2.1, where the mass transfer
kinetics are assumed to be inﬁnitely fast. To support, validate and asses
the results within a broader class of more complex scenarios, the proposed
==PAGE:119==
5.2 Recommendations for Future Work
105
state estimation techniques should be applied to the more complex models,
such as the LKM or GRM with ﬁnite mass transfer kinetics. The work
put forward in this thesis could naturally serve as a ﬁrst step towards
these models. A natural discretization choice of the GRM would be the
DG-FE method. However, specialized open-source software as CADET
[Lv18], high-ﬁdelity ﬁnite-element based simulators such as FEniCS1 or
the Spectral/hp Element Framework NEKTAR++2 could be investigated
as potential accurate and eﬃcient simulators for the nonlinear, convection-
dominated models.
• With promising results this thesis has applied state estimation of the
uncertain inlet concentrations to the EDM. However, as suggested in
[LNE14, KE06, KDS+09a, AC03] the parameters of the EDM are inher-
ently uncertain and require estimation for each chromatographic process.
Future work should therefore seek to investigate diﬀerent parameter es-
timation methods for the linear and nonlinear EDM potentially using
Kalman ﬁlter technology.
Parameter estimation methods could for ex-
ample be the nonlinear ﬁlters: EKF, UKF, EnKF and PF. Experimental
data could be investigated to estimate the key parameters in the EDM,
and apply the ﬁlters on actual data instead of the synthetic data, which
is constructed from a model with known parameters.
• The work in this thesis considers a simple LSERK method for time inte-
gration. Explicit Runge-Kutta methods such as the LSERK are popular
for integrating semi-discrete systems [HW08] resulting from DG-FE dis-
cretizations. However, these methods suﬀer from stability based time step
restrictions for stiﬀproblems with convection-dominated fronts. Implicit-
explicit integration schemes for the EDM exist [MHA18a, DGM18, BMRS18],
and these methods are able to capture shocks, which are given rise to by
sharp moving transitions between concentrations. These methods do not
compromise the accuracy of the solution, and they allow the solution to
be propagated at larger time-steps than conventional methods. This has
potential to reduce simulation time of the models. Future work should
therefore seek to investigate the beneﬁts of using such methods.
• The work in this thesis does not consider control of the chromatographic
process. Based on this work however, a natural extension would be to
consider MPC of packed bed chromatographic process. A counter-current
continuous process has been studied in [DAPC15], which controls the pro-
cess by a periodical switching of the inlet and outlet ports in the direction
of the ﬂuid ﬂow.
However, literature does not currently suggest study
of NMPC for conventional batch chromatography, and there are diﬀerent
perspectives on how the control process could be modeled. Possibilities
1Website: https://fenicsproject.org/
2Website: https://www.nektar.info/features/
==PAGE:120==
106
Outlook
include: 1) manipulating the concentrations by injection of an extra com-
ponent (such as solutes of salt) to manipulate the aﬃnities of the other
components. 2) Indirectly modeling the change in component aﬃnities
through the equilibrium parameters in the isotherms, by assuming that
ai = ai(t) and bi = bi(t) in the Langmuir isotherm (2.27) vary over time.
• The DG-FE method and nonlinear ﬁlters developed in this thesis have been
implemented for models of the form (2.4). The U-Loop reactor [OJVJ10]
is an example of a process, that may be described by a model that is
equivalent, and shares similar structure with (2.4). It would therefore be
of interest to extend the methodologies developed in this work to other
models, such as the U-Loop reactor, which shares similar structure, and
likewise to other chemical processes, which may be modeled as convection-
diﬀusion processes.

==PAGE:125==
A p p e n d i x B
Project Plan and Evaluation
This appendix contains the original project plan for the thesis, which we devel-
oped in the onset of the project, and is presented in the following. Furthermore
the project plan has been extended with an evaluation of the workload during
the project.
Project background: Chromatography is a widely used chemical technique
to separate mixtures or components in chemical plants. It is used for separating
mixtures into components in order to analyze, identify, purify, quantify, and
optimize. Chromatographic separation processes are based on the diﬀerential
adsorption of the components of a mixture. In conventional liquid chromatog-
raphy, the separation is achieved by injecting a pulse of the solute mixture into
the chromatographic column. The components move with diﬀerent velocities in
the column due to their diﬀerent aﬃnity with the solid phase. Thus, the low
retained component exits the column earlier than the more retained one and,
hence, the separation is achieved.
Motivation and challenges: The success of the separation process is primar-
ily tied to the fulﬁllment of two objectives: 1) to extract as much as possible of a
given component in 2) the least amount of time. As a challenge, these objectives
are in mutual conﬂict. To optimize the separation process, it becomes natural to
consider appropriate control strategies such a Nonlinear Model Predictive Con-
trol (NMPC). However, as a fundamental prerequisite, NMPC and other control
==PAGE:126==
112
Project Plan and Evaluation
techniques rely crucially on proper state estimation. For chromatographic pro-
cesses, there are a number of challenges. Firstly, the process is described by
coupled nonlinear partial diﬀerential equations (PDEs) that upon discretiza-
tion leads to a large number of states.
In contrast, there are typically only
a few measurements available to base the estimation on. Secondly, the initial
concentrations are often highly uncertain to the point where any optimization
procedure becomes corrupted.
Project goals and description: To meet the above challenges, this project
seeks to develop fast and reliable state estimation routines for the chromato-
graphic process. To this end, the project falls into two main parts:
(i) Fast simulation of chromatographic processes: The simulation of mul-
ticomponent chromatographic processes under nonlinear conditions and reaction
kinetics require fast and accurate numerical methods. A fundamental feature
of the ﬁrst order nonlinear partial diﬀerential equations is that discontinuities
in the solutions can develop shocks that cause major diﬃculties for the numer-
ical schemes. To this end, projects aims to develop fast, iterative discontinuous
Galerkin solvers tailored for simulation of multicomponent chromatographic pro-
cesses in 1D and 2D. DG-FE methods allow for discontinuous approximations
and produce block-diagonal mass matrices that can be used to construct eﬃ-
cient algorithms of low computational cost. These methods incorporate the idea
of numerical ﬂuxes and slope limiters in a very natural way to avoid spurious
oscillations, which usually occur due the sharp changes in the solution. The de-
veloped solvers are intended to serve as a ﬁrst step towards a realistic, parallel
3D solver, capable of handling complex geometries. Furthermore, the solvers
serve as a prerequisite for the second part of the project that concerns state
estimation.
==PAGE:127==
113
(ii) State estimation: The second part of the project seeks to develop proce-
dures for eﬃcient state estimation. Based on real-life measurements, the project
serves to investigate and adapt current state-of-the-art methodologies to ﬁt the
framework of the chromatographic process.
The goal is to asses the poten-
tial of each ﬁlter in terms of eﬀectiveness and computational eﬃciency. The
project will focus on the following ﬁltering techniques for nonlinear processes:
1) Extended Kalman Filter (EKF), 2) the Unscented Kalman Filter (UKF) 3)
Particle Filters (PF), and 4) Ensemble Kalman Filter (EnKF). To ensure fast
estimation, the project seeks to implement the ﬁlters in parallel using the DTU
HPC Cluster.
In summation, the project will follow the four steps set out:
1. Gain an in-depth understanding of the chromatographic process and the
underlying partial diﬀerential equations describing the model.
2. Develop iterative DG-FE solvers in 1D and 2D for fast and accurate sim-
ulations of the physical model.
3. Investigate state estimation algorithms and apply to the nonlinear simu-
lator alongside real data.
4. Document the investigation and results in a comprehensive thesis.
Outline: As a way of estimating the amount of work required to progress
through each step of the project, we have have outlined a plan for the proposed
workload during the semester. The project begins at the 27th of August and the
thesis is scheduled for hand-in by the 27th of January. The following outlines
a tentative plan for the workload set through the coming semester. This plan
is not compulsory, and is open to restructuring, but is key for giving an idea of
how well the project progresses.
August: The last week of August and ﬁrst week of September, will be spent on
doing a literature search of relevant research articles and books, which should
help with providing a solid background for understanding the basics of the non-
linear equilibrium dispersive model of chromatography and nodal discontinuous-
Galerkin ﬁnite-element methods (DG-FE). This background should then help
towards a theoretical understanding of the PDE model and result in a practical
implementation of a DG-FE to solve the conservation problem.
September: The ﬁrst three weeks of September will be spent on gaining an
understanding of how to derive and implement basic DG-FE schemes from rele-
vant literature. The idea is to split the nonlinear equilibrium dispersive model of
==PAGE:128==
114
Project Plan and Evaluation
chromatography into its respective convective, dispersive and nonlinear terms,
and gain an understanding of the methods by solving each term separately in a
progressive manner. We would begin by solving the linear convection equation
and continue by adding dispersive, nonlinear and coupling terms, instead of at-
tempting to solve the original problem headﬁrst. This should give a thorough
understanding that will be useful in implementing the actual DG-FE solver.
October: The four weeks of October (and part of November) will be spent on
implementing a fast, iterative DG-FE simulator of chromatographic processes
eﬃciently in 1D (possibly 2D). The goal is to have a solver capable of doing
fast simulations of chromatographic processes for multiple components ready
by then.
November: Part of October and in the beginning weeks of November will
be spent on researching and gaining an understanding of the four nonlinear
state estimation algorithms that is of interest in this project. The last part of
November will be to apply each ﬁlter algorithms on a synthetic test case of the
nonlinear chromatographic solver with added noise.
December: The last part of the project begins in the remaining weeks of
December will be to investigate real life data obtained from actual experiments
and apply the ﬁlter algorithms in combination with the nonlinear solver to
perform a state estimation and uncertainty quantiﬁcation of the problem.
January: The last part of January will be devoted to making sure that the
relevant data has been acquired and that the simulations and tests have been
performed to a satisfactory extent. Also the remaining time will be spent on
ﬁnalizing the report.
During each month the plan is to document as much as possible in the thesis,
such that the remaining report writing in January will not become as stressful
as possibly anticipated. Finally, as a way to structure the workload through
each month Figure B.1 shows a Gantt-chart of the distribution of work through
each week during the autumn semester.
Auto-Evaluation: To a large extent the proposed project outline in Figure
B.1 has been followed. Motivated by early positive results, the idea of extending
the DG-FE simulator to 2D was abandoned in favor of working on the paper:
Discontinuous-Galerkin Finite-Element Method for Simulation of Packed Bed
Chromatographic Processes. Two weeks of October were used to complete the
paper. The paper was submitted and later accepted. November month was
spent on developing the Kalman ﬁlter for the linear EDM using the FV and
DG-FE method.
Positive results based on this work led to the paper: Spa-
tial Discretization and Kalman Filtering for Ideal Packed-Bed Chromatography.

A p p e n d i x C
Paper I
A Discontinuous-Galerkin Finite-Element Method for Sim-
ulation of Packed Bed Chromatographic Processes
Authors: Alexander Hørsholt, Lasse Hjuler Christiansen, Kristian Meyer, Jakob
Kjøbstad Huusom, John Bagterp Jørgensen.
Submitted to: The 12th IFAC Symposium on Dynamics and Control of Pro-
cess Systems, including Biosystems (DYCOPS 2019).
==PAGE:132==
A Discontinuous-Galerkin
Finite-Element Method for Simulation of
Packed Bed Chromatographic Processes
A. Hørsholt ∗, L.H. Christiansen ∗, K. Meyer ∗∗,
J.K. Huusom ∗∗, J.B. Jørgensen ∗
∗Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science
& Center for Energy Resources Engineering (CERE),
Technical University of Denmark, DK-2800 Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark
(e-mails: {aho, lhch, jbo}@dtu.dk)
∗∗Process and Systems Engineering Centre (PROSYS),
Department of Chemical Engineering,
Technical University of Denmark, DK-2800 Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark
(e-mails: {krismey, jkh}@kt.dtu.dk)
Abstract: Packed bed chromatography is an important unit operation for puriﬁcation of
product molecules in biopharmaceutical processes. Packed bed chromatographic processes
are modeled as advection-diﬀusion-reaction partial diﬀerential equations. The advection term
strongly dominates the diﬀusion term. Therefore, specialized numerical methods must be used
for eﬃcient simulation of packed bed chromatographic processes. In this paper, we use a
discontinuous-Galerkin method on ﬁnite-elements for spatial discretization and low storage
explicit Runge-Kutta (LSERK) methods for numerical solution of the resulting system of
diﬀerential equations. We study the numerical solution of deterministic and stochastic models of
packed bed chromatographic processes. The stochastic model and its corresponding numerical
solution constitute the ﬁrst step toward systematic monitoring, fault detection, and optimal
predictive control of chromatographic processes. It is also an essential ingredient in uncertainty
quantiﬁcation for eﬃcient and robust design and operation of chromatographic processes.
Keywords: Chromatography, Mathematical Modeling, Numerical Simulation, Biotechnology.
1. INTRODUCTION
Even though chromatography is a costly separation pro-
cess, it is extensively used in downstream biopharmaceu-
tical manufacturing (Hong et al., 2018). Consequently,
mathematical models for simulation, parameter estima-
tion, design, control, and optimization of chromatographic
processes are important. The modeling (Arkell et al.,
2018), simulation (Klatt et al., 2000; von Lieres and An-
dersson, 2010; He et al., 2018; Leweke and von Lieres,
2018; Borg et al., 2013), control (Klatt et al., 2002), and
optimization (P¨uttmann et al., 2013; Holmqvist and Mag-
nusson, 2016; Sellberg, 2018) of chromatographic processes
have been extensively studied. Observers for chromato-
graphic procseses have also been studied (Alamir and
Corriou, 2003; Corriou and Alamir, 2006; K¨upper and En-
gell, 2006; K¨upper et al., 2009; Lemoine-Nava and Engell,
2014). Also many processes such as adsorption possess
structural similarities to chromatography, such that the
numerical techniques for observers in such processes can
be transfered to chromatographic processes (Won and Lee,
2012). In this paper, we use high-order methods for eﬃ-
cient numerical simulation of the advection-diﬀusion par-
⋆This work is part of ”Model based energy eﬃcient control for the
process industries” that is funded by EUDP in the project 64017-
05136.
tial diﬀerential equation that models chromatographic pro-
cesses (Hesthaven and Warburton, 2008; Lord et al., 2014).
We apply this method to handle stochastic inlet boundary
conditions as a ﬁrst step toward extended and unscented
Kalman ﬁlters for chromatographic processes. Systematic
use of such ﬁlters allows for monitoring, prediciton and
fault detection of chromatographic processes. The special-
ized methods, that we use for simulation in this paper, are
also needed in the extended and unscented Kalman ﬁlters
for continuous-discrete systems.
1.1 Chromatographic Processes
Chromatography is widely used for separation of pep-
tides and proteins in biopharmaceutical processes (Gui-
chon et al., 2006). Analytically, chromatography is used
for separating mixtures into components in order to an-
alyze, identify, purify, quantify, and optimize proceses
(Holmqvist and Magnusson, 2016). In production scale, it
is used to isolate and purify the compound of interest. Fig.
1 illustrates a chromatographic column. In liquid column
chromatography a cylindrical column is packed with a
stationary porous material. A continuous ﬂow of liquid
with more components are sent to the inlet of the column
in a so-called mobile phase. As illustrated in Fig. 1, this
creates a ﬂow that moves through the column from the
left to the right. The injected liquid contains the mixture
==PAGE:133==

Fig. 1. Conceptual diagram of packed bed chromato-
graphic separation column and the separation prin-
ciple.
of components. The physical and chemical properties of
each component lead to diﬀerent aﬃnities of the molecules
to the mobile and the stationary phase. This diﬀerence
is used to separate the molecules by having them move
along the column at diﬀerent speeds. Typically, a known
solute with a strong aﬃnity to the stationary phase is
injected to manipulate pH, polarity or another property
that inﬂuences the separation of the molecules of interest.
1.2 Motivation
The simulation of multicomponent chromatographic pro-
cesses under non-linear conditions and reaction kinetics
require fast and accurate numerical methods (Javeed et al.,
2011; He et al., 2018; Meyer et al., 2018). These pro-
cesses are typically described by parabolic, non-linear,
convection-dominated, coupled partial diﬀerential equa-
tions (Javeed et al., 2011). Due to the underlying physics,
these partial diﬀerential equations are advection domi-
nated such that discontinuities in the inputs produces
shocks on either side of the elution bands (Ma and Guio-
chon, 1992). These shocks challenge and cause diﬃculties
for many numerical methods based on ﬁnite-diﬀerence
or ﬁnite volume discretization. To mitigate this, we use
a high-order nodal discontinuous-Galerkin ﬁnite-element
(DG-FE) scheme as an accurate and cost eﬃcient simula-
tor (Meyer et al., 2018). We show the h- and p-adaptivity
of the scheme by increasing the number of elements for a
ﬁxed polynomial order or by increasing the polynomial
order for a ﬁxed number of elements (Hesthaven and
Warburton, 2008). This validates that the method has the
expected high order of accuracy.
Typically, the inlet consist of many complex molecules
that are produced by a fermentation in the upstream
biopharmaceutical manufacturing process. Often the inlet
concentration of these molelcules are not measured, and
the concentrations themselves may vary signiﬁcantly. This
signiﬁcant uncertainty in the inlet concentrations makes it
hard to predict the states of a chromatographic process.
Eﬃcient state estimators may mitigate this uncertainty
by using the available measurements at the outlet. Such
estimators require a stochastic model. In this paper, we
develop a deterministic model and use it with piecewise
constant stochastic inputs. This combination produces a
stochastic model that addresses the impact that highly
uncertain inlet concentrations have on the outlet concen-
trations. In a numerical case study, we simulate the deter-
ministic model using the DG-FE simulator with the inlet
concentrations being normally distributed and piecewise
constant. The results demonstrate the eﬀect that uncertain
inlet concentrations have on the simulated states at the
outlet and emphasize the need to mitigate this eﬀect by
eﬃcient state estimation. This case study also represents
a ﬁrst-step towards observers based on extended and un-
scented Kalman ﬁlter technology.
1.3 Paper Organization
This paper is organized as follows. Section 2 presents the
equilibrium dispersive model for chromatography that we
study in this paper. Section 3 describes the numerical
discretization of the model by the DG-FE method. Section
4 presents the numerical results and conclusions are made
in Section 5.
2. THE CHROMATOGRAPHIC MODEL
The literature contains a variety of models that describes
the behaviour of liquid chromatographic processes inside
a separation column. Well known models include the
equilibrium dispersive model, the lumped kinetic model,
and the general rate model (Guichon et al., 2006). These
models are non-linear, convection dominated partial diﬀer-
ential equations describing the interaction between chro-
matographic components inside a separation column, each
under varying assumptions. In this work, we consider
the equilibrium dispersive model (ED). The ED model is
derived on six fundamental assumptions (Qamar et al.,
2013): 1) The chromatographic process occurring in the
separation column is isothermal. 2) The column is radially
homogeneous. 3) The stationary phase of the column is
packed with a porous material of equally sized spherical
particles. 4) The mobile phase is incompressible. 5) The
mobile and stationary phase of each component do not
interact with each other. 6) There is axial dispersion in
the process, which causes band broadening of the concen-
tration proﬁles.
2.1 The Equilibrium Dispersive Model of Chromatography
The ED model is a partial diﬀerential equation of the form
(1a) and involves the concentrations of Nc components in
the mobile, ci, and stationary phase, qi. The transition
from mobile to stationary phase is modelled by an equi-
librium adsorption isotherm. In the literature, diﬀerent
boundary conditions exist to model almost any conﬁgu-
ration of the column (Guichon et al., 2006). A Danckwerts
boundary condition is used to model the inlet concentra-
tion proﬁle and a zero Neumann condition is given at the
outlet (Danckwerts, 1953). Given proper initial conditions,
the multi-component ED model is given as

2.2 The Equilibrium Isotherm
The equilibrium isotherm describes the distribution of a
component between the mobile and the stationary phase
in the porous media. This implies that in general qi is a
function qi = f(c1, c2, . . . , ci, . . . , cNc) depending on all the
components in the chromatographic column. Therefore f is
typically a non-linear function that couples the equations
in (1).
The competitive Langmuir adsorption isotherm describes
a non-linear interaction between components as (Guichon
et al., 2006)

For low concentrations the competition between compo-
nents become almost negligible and (3) typically simpliﬁes
to the linear isotherm (4).

3. DISCRETIZATION
The model equations (7) are solved using a method-
of-lines approach. This procedure allows for a separate
discretization of the spatial and temporal variables. The
spatial discretization uses a DG-FEM method (Meyer
et al., 2018). This discretization recovers a continuous-
time-discrete-space system. This semi-discrete system (27)
is discretized in time using a low storage, ﬁve stage, fourth
order explicit Runge-Kutta (LSERK) method (Hesthaven
and Warburton, 2008).

The DG-FEM method allows for a practical implementa-
tion of the boundary conditions, which is done weakly by
deﬁning the exterior ghost states (c+, g+) at the left and
right most boundary of Ω. The Robin boundary condition
(1b) at the inlet is implemented by requiring that

produce a set of ODEs that are solved using an LSERK
method.

the following considers two case studies. To verify the DG-
FEM scheme, the ﬁrst study performs a convergence test
for the linear isotherm (4) that compares the error between
the known solution given by van Genuchten (1981) and the

4.1 Case I: One component, linear isotherm
This case study demonstrates numerical convergence of
the DG-FEM scheme by verifying the h\p-adaptivity of
the error, which is deﬁned by the diﬀerence between
the numerical and analytical solution, i.e., ea = c −ca.

polynomial order, N, is constant and the mesh is adjusted.
In p-adaptivity, Ne is kept constant while the polynomial
order is increased on each element. In particular, p- adap-
tivity proves superior to h- adaptivity in terms of DOFs
and accuracy.

5. CONCLUSION
This paper presents a high-order DG-FE method for ef-
ﬁcient and accurate simulation of chromatographic pro-
cesses that can be described by the ED model. This is mo-
tivated by the observation that high-order methods should
be considered when simulating convection-dominated non-
linear partial diﬀerential equations such as (1). As the
main contribution, this paper considers the eﬀects of un-
certainties in the inlet concentrations. The stochastic simu-
lation demonstrates signiﬁcant deviations between the de-
terministic and stochastic states and outlet concentrations.
The stochastic chromatographic simulation model is a ﬁrst
step towards observers based on extended and unscented
Kalman ﬁlter technology.

==PAGE:139==
A p p e n d i x D
Paper II
Spatial Discretization and Kalman Filtering for Ideal Packed-
Bed Chromatography
Authors: Alexander Hørsholt, Lasse Hjuler Christiansen, Kristian Meyer, Jakob
Kjøbstad Huusom, John Bagterp Jørgensen.
Submitted to: The 17th European Control Conference (ECC19).
==PAGE:140==
Spatial Discretization and Kalman Filtering
for Ideal Packed-Bed Chromatography
Alexander Hørsholt, Lasse Hjuler Christiansen, Kristian Meyer, Jakob Kjøbsted Huusom, John Bagterp Jørgensen
Abstract— Packed-bed
chromatography
is
ubiquitous
in
pharmaceutical and biotechnological manufacturing for sep-
aration of peptides, proteins, and antibiotic molecules. We
investigate a mathematical model for ideal chromatography in
a packed bed. The partial differential equation system that
constitutes the model is linear. We discretized the mathematical
model spatially using a ﬁrst-order, ﬁnite-volume (FV) method
as well as a high-order, discontinuous-Galerkin ﬁnite-element
(DG-FE) method. We use an exact temporal discretization of
the resulting system of linear equations. For the same accuracy,
the DG-FE method requires far less states than the FV method.
We use the resulting discrete-time, state-space model for state
estimation of the packed-bed chromatographic model using a
Kalman ﬁlter. By simulation, we illustrate the applications of
the resulting state estimator in a monitoring and prediction
system for packed-bed chromatography.
I. INTRODUCTION
Column liquid chromatography is an important and widely
used unit operation for downstream processing in the bio-
pharmaceutical industry [1]. With a global biopharmaceutical
market predicted to be 167 billion US Dollars by 2015
[2], the potential mathematical modeling, parameter estima-
tion, control and optimization can have on chromatographic
processes is therefore becoming increasingly signiﬁcant.
Deterministic modeling [3], parameter estimation [4], [5],
optimization and control [6] of chromatographic processes
have been extensively studied. However, for chromatographic
processes there are a number of challenges. Experimental
applications typically contain uncertain inlet concentrations
or parameters [7]. Measurements of the inlet concentrations
are seldomly possible. Information of the process is therefore
limited to the measurements taken at the outlet, which only
begin to become available after the process has begun and
are typically corrupted by measurement noise [8]. A high
workload is often associated with an experimental setup of a
chromatographic process [9] and while modeling provides
a technique for reducing experimental effort, it becomes
necessary to address the inherent uncertainties in the process.
The purpose of state estimation is to reconstruct the state
of a system from process measurements given a model de-
scription. State estimation is ubiquitous in non-linear model
predictive control and as an estimator in monitoring and
*A. Hørsholt, Lasse Hjuler Christiansen and J. B. Jørgensen are with the
Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science, Technical Uni-
versity of Denmark, DK-2800 Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark. K. Meyer and J.K.
Huusom are with Department of Chemical and Biochemical Engineering,
Technical University of Denmark, DK-2800 Kgs. Lyngby. Corresponding
author: J. B. Jørgensen (E-mail: jbjo@dtu.dk)
prediction of chemical processes [10]. Given a physical
model describing the chromatographic process, state estima-
tion provides a way to mitigate the inherent uncertainties.
In this paper, we develop a linear-time, state-space model
based on a ﬁrst-order, ﬁnite-volume (FV) and a high-order,
discontinuous-Galerkin ﬁnite-element (DG-FE) method [11],
[12] for computationally efﬁcient numerical simulation of the
advection-diffusion partial differential equation that models
the chromatographic process [13]. We present a stochas-
tic, linear-time, state-space model with piecewise noise to
model a stochastic inlet boundary condition. We develop a
disturbance model to model the unknown input concentra-
tion and present a Kalman ﬁlter to estimate the unknown
inlet concentration and states simultaneously based on noisy
concentration measurements at the outlet.
The paper is organized as follows: Section II presents
the Equilibrium Dispersive (ED) model of chromatography,
we study in this paper. Section III describes the numerical
discretization of the model by the FV and DG-FE method.
Section IV presents the stochastic, time-linear state-space
model and describes the Kalman ﬁlter. Section V presents
the numerical results and conclusions are made in Section
VI.
II. MATHEMATICAL MODEL FOR CHROMATOGRAPHY
Fig. 1 illustrates the principles of molecular separation
using chromatography. In this section, we present a math-
ematical model for a packed-bed chromatographic column.
The key assumption in this model is that the stationary and
mobile phase of the column is in equilibrium and that this
equilibrium is ideal.
A. The Equilibrium Dispersive Model
The Equilibrium Dispersive (ED) Model is a convection-
dominated, non-linear partial differential equation of the
form (1) and governs the ﬂow of Nc components in the
mobile, ci, and stationary, qi, phases. The ED model is based
on six fundamental assumptions: 1) The chromatographic
process is isothermal and the transition from mobile to
stationary phase is modeled by an equilibrium isotherm
with instantaneous reaction kinetics. 2) The mobile phase is
incompressible. 3) There is axial dispersion in the column,
which causes band broadening. 4) The stationary phase
is packed with a porous material of equidistant spherical
particles. 5) The column is radially homogeneous. 6) The
mobile and stationary phase of each component do not

III. NUMERICAL METHODS
The model equations are discretized using two approaches:
1) A ﬁrst-order accurate, ﬁnite-volume (FV) discretization
and 2) a high-order accurate, nodal discontinuous-Galerkin
ﬁnite-element (DG-FE) method [11], [12]. This discretization
leads to a linear, continuous-time, discrete-state formulation,
which may solved by an exact temporal discretization to
recover a discrete-time, state-space model.

IV. KALMAN FILTERING
The following introduces the stochastic, time-linear, state-
space model. The dynamic and stationary Kalman state
estimation ﬁlters associated to the state-space model are pre-
sented. Given a linear-time, state-space model the stationary
Kalman ﬁlter is the best linear estimator and several of the
matrices are considered static and may be precomputed [18].
A. Stochastic Linear State Space Model
The linear discrete-time stochastic system is
xk+1 = Axk + Buk + Gwk,
(29a)
zk = Cxk,
(29b)
yk = zk + vk.
(29c)
This corresponds to conversion from a continuous-time rep-
resentation to an equivalent discrete-time representation. The
initial state, x0, process, wk, and measurement noise, vk, are
normally distributed as
x0 ∼N(¯x0, P0),
(30a)
wk ∼Niid(0, Q), vk ∼Niid(0, R),
(30b)
with covariances P0, Q and R.
B. Dynamic Kalman Filter
Given that the mean and covariance of the initial state
(30a) are known. The dynamical Kalman ﬁlter equations may
be stated as follows. The one-step ahead prediction of the
state and its covariance are computed as
ˆxk|k−1 = Aˆxk−1|k−1 + Buk−1,
(31a)
Pk|k−1 = APk−1|k−1A′ + GQG′.
(31b)
The one-step ahead prediction of the measurement is com-
puted as
ˆyk|k−1 = Cˆxk|k−1.
(32)
The innovation, ek, is obtained as
ek = yk −ˆyk|k−1,
(33a)
Re,k = CPk|k−1C′ + R,
(33b)
and the state ﬁlter gain is computed by the expression
Kfx,k = Pk|k−1C′R−1
e,k.
(34)
The ﬁltered state, ˆxk|k, and its covariance, Pk|k, are com-
puted as
ˆxk|k = ˆxk|k−1 + Kfx,kek,
(35a)
Pk|k = Pk|k−1 −Kfx,kRe,kK′
fx,k.
(35b)
Remark 3: To ensure that the covariance, Pk|k, is positive
semideﬁnite (35b) is rewritten as
Pk|k = (I −Kfx,kC)Pk|k−1(I −Kfx,kC)′
+ Kfx,kRK′
fx,k,
(36)
since Pk|k−1 and R are positive semideﬁnite.
C. Stationary Kalman Filter
The stationary Kalman ﬁlter is a computationally more
attractive alternative to the dynamic, since the matrices may
be precomputed ofﬂine and it provides the best state-estimate
for linear systems.
The covariance, P, is obtained by solving the discrete
algebraic Ricatti equation
P = APA′ + GQG′ −(APC′)(CPC′ + R)−1(APC′)′,
(37)
which is used to compute the stationary state and the ﬁlter
gains
Re = CPC′ + R,
(38a)
Kfx = PC′R−1
e .
(38b)
The innovation, ek, and ﬁltered state, ˆxk|k, are computed as
ˆxk|k−1 = Aˆxk−1|k−1 + Buk−1,
(39a)
ˆyk|k−1 = Cˆxk|k−1,
(39b)
ek = yk −ˆyk|k−1,
(39c)
ˆxk|k = ˆxk|k−1 + Kfxek.
(39d)
The covariance, Pk|k, is computed as
Pk|k = (I −KfxC)P(I −KfxC)′ + KfxRK′
fx.
(40)
D. Prediction
In the linear-time case the states are predicted as
ˆxk+1+j|k = Aˆxk+j|k + Bˆuk+j|k, j > 0,
(41a)
Pk+1+j|k = APk+j|kA′ + GQG′.
(41b)
The output prediction is
ˆzk+j|k = Cˆxk+j|k,
j > 0,
(42a)
Rz,k+j|k = CPk+j|kC′.
(42b)

V. SIMULATION RESULTS
The numerical case study consists of two parts: 1) To
demonstrate the capability of using high-order methods, the
ﬁrst study compares simulation and ﬁltered state perfor-
mance of the FV and DG-FE method in terms of accuracy,
degrees of freedom and simulation time. 2) The second
study demonstrates the use of the augmented model (45)
in estimating the unknown input under varying inﬂuence of
the disturbance tuning Qξ. The column parameters for both
studies are listed in Table I and resemble a realistic study
in [16]. The concentrations are modiﬁed to avoid unphysical
negative concentrations.
A. Case Comparison: FV vs. DG-FE Method
Figure 3 compares the performance of the FV and DG-FE
method relative to a desired accuracy ϵ. The error is measured
in the standard ℓ2(Ω) norm

concentrate was injected at tinj = 2 minutes. The time is

Fig. 4: Comparison of the desired tolerance achievable by
the DG-FE and FV method for varying CPU time and DOFs
when estimating the ﬁltered states using the dynamic Kalman

discretized using a sampling time Ts = 0.1 minutes resulting
in k = 20 iterations. The solution is stopped while the
injected concentration band remains inside the column, such
that the L2 error may be evaluated exactly. The FV method
is simulated using Nz ∈[200, 400, . . . , 4000] DOFs and the
DG-FE is simulated using K = 30 elements and polynomial
orders of P ∈[1, 2, . . . , 14].
Figure 3 shows that the DG-FE method offers higher
order accuracy at signiﬁcantly fewer states and computational
effort, when compared to the FV method. This is due
to the spectral convergence property, which is tied to the
smoothness of the solution [13]. For the same accuracy, the
DG-FE method requires far less states than the FV method.
For engineering solutions, such a level of accuracy displayed
by the DG-FE method is typically unnecessary, but seeks to
motivate the use of high-order methods.
Figure 4 compares the performance of the FV and DG-FE
method when estimating the inlet concentration using the
disturbance model with noise covariances Q = R = 0.12
and Qξ = 0.0012 and the dynamic Kalman ﬁlter with initial
covariance matrix set to the unity P0 = I. As expected from
Figure 3 the DG-FE method is signiﬁcantly faster compared
to the FV method in computing the estimated states.
Remark 4: Note, that for increasing number of DOFs,
the matrix exponential becomes increasingly time consuming
to compute. The FV method requires many states to reach
high accuracy and therefore the additional cost of computing
the matrix exponential becomes signiﬁcant. However, this is
not included in the timing, since the matrix exponential is
considered an ofﬂine computation.

Figure 5 displays the estimated ﬁltered input and output
states for varying disturbance tuning Qξ using the dynamic
Kalman ﬁlter and initial covariance matrix set to the unity
P0 = I. The input is estimated after a natural time delay of
approximately 3 minutes, since the measurements only begin
to appear at the outlet of the column after this period. For
low values of the tuning parameter, the inlet concentration
is estimated as a smooth transition. Increasing the value of
the tuning parameter allows the estimate to capture more of
the discontinuity. However, this is compromised by a more
aggressive estimate, as seen in Figure 5, and naturally a
trade-off in this transition occurs.
VI. CONCLUSION
We have presented a high-order numerical model for
simulation and state estimation of the linear chromatography
problem (10). For the same accuracy, the high-order model
requires far less states than the FV model, and provides fast
and efﬁcient simultaneous estimation of input and output
under uncertain inlet concentrations. The model used for
state estimation represent the ﬁrst steps towards closed loop
control and optimization of the chromatographic process.

132

==PAGE:147==
A p p e n d i x E
Paper III
State and Input Estimation of Nonlinear Chromatographic
Processes
Authors: Alexander Hørsholt, Lasse Hjuler Christiansen, Tobias Kasper Ritschel,
Kristian Meyer, Jakob Kjøbstad Huusom, John Bagterp Jørgensen.
Submitted to: 3rd IEEE Conference on Control Technology and Applications
(CCTA 2019).
==PAGE:148==
State and Input Estimation of Nonlinear Chromatographic Processes
Alexander Hørsholt, Lasse Hjuler Christiansen, Tobias Kasper Ritschel, Kristian Meyer,
Jakob Kjøbsted Huusom, John Bagterp Jørgensen
Abstract— We investigate two algorithms for state estimation
of nonlinear chromatographic processes: the Extended Kalman
Filter and the Ensemble Kalman Filter. We consider the
Equilibrium Dispersive Model for modeling of packed-bed chro-
matography. The Equilibrium Dispersive Model is governed
by a convection-dominated nonlinear partial differential equa-
tion. The model is discretized by a high-order discontinuous-
Galerkin ﬁnite-element method for accurate and efﬁcient simu-
lation of the chromatographic process. The discretization leads
to a stochastic continuous-discrete model, and we use the
Extended Kalman Filter and the Ensemble Kalman Filter for
state estimation of the packed-bed chromatographic model. The
performance and capabilities of both ﬁlters are demonstrated
in simultaneous estimation of the unknown system states and
uncertain inlet concentration.
I. INTRODUCTION
Packed-bed chromatography is a ubiquitous unit-operation
for downstream processing of biopharmaceutical products
[1]. Practical application of chromatography is a costly
procedure [2] and modeling of chromatographic processes is
an active and extensively studied ﬁeld [3]–[6]. The success
of the separation process is tied to a number of challenges,
among these are: 1) Complex models of chromatographic
processes often require independent determination of many
uncertain model parameters, which may be be costly and
difﬁcult to derive by experiments [7]–[10]. 2) Measurements
of the inlet concentration are typically not possible to obtain
for industrial feed stocks, and information of the process is
therefore entirely tied to the uncertain measurements taken
at the outlet [11]. Before considering optimal control and
optimization [12] of chromatographic processes, it becomes
necessary to address the inherent uncertainties in said pro-
cess.
State estimation is the reconstruction of system states
based on measurements and is a key component in model
predictive control, parameter estimation, monitoring and
prediction [13]. State estimation is widely applied for many
nonlinear chemical processes [14]. Moving horizon state and
parameter estimation has been applied to chromatographic
processes to estimate the adsorption isotherm parameters
[15], [16] and for nonlinear MPC of chromatographic pro-
cesses [17].
There exist a variety of different state estimation algo-
rithms (ﬁlters) for nonlinear systems, among these are: 1)
*A. Hørsholt, L. H. Christiansen, T. K. Ritschel and J. B. Jørgensen
are with the Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science,
Technical University of Denmark, DK-2800 Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark. K.
Meyer and J.K. Huusom are with Department of Chemical and Biochemical
Engineering, Technical University of Denmark, DK-2800 Kgs. Lyngby.
Corresponding author: J. B. Jørgensen (E-mail: jbjo@dtu.dk)
The Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) and 2) the Ensemble
Kalman Filter (EnKF). The EKF linearizes the dynamics of
the nonlinear model and applies the original Kalman ﬁlter
equations [18]. The EnKF is a particular type of particle
ﬁlter, which uses the Kalman ﬁlter equations and a recursive
least-squares update, based on an ensemble to capture the
probability distribution of the uncertain states [19]. The
ensemble size is a tuning parameter of the EnKF.
In this paper we present the EKF and EnKF for
state estimation of nonlinear chromatographic processes.
The nonlinear chromatographic process is modeled by
the Equilibrium Dispersive Model (EDM) with a non-
linear Langmuir isotherm. The EDM is discretized by a
high-order discontinuous-Galerkin ﬁnite-element (DG-FE)
method [20]–[22] for accurate and efﬁcient numerical sim-
ulation of the governing convection-dominated partial dif-
ferential equations [23]. We investigate a comparative study
of the EKF and EnKF for state estimation. In addition we
demonstrate the capabilities of both ﬁlters for estimating
the uncertain inlet concentrations and states simultaneously
based on noisy measurements at the outlet.
The paper is organized as follows: Section II introduces
the EDM. Section III describes the numerical discretization
of the model by the DG-FE method. Section IV introduces
the stochastic continuous-discrete model which follows from
a spatial discretization of the EDM. Section V describes the
continuous-discrete EKF and EnKF for estimation of the
unknown inlet concentration. Section VI presents a numerical
case study, which compares the performance of the EKF and
EnKF and their capability for estimating the unknown inlet
concentration. Conclusions are given in Section VII.
II. MATHEMATICAL MODEL FOR CHROMATOGRAPHY
Figure 1 shows a conceptual representation of the molec-
ular separation process which occurs in packed-bed chro-
matography. A continuous ﬂow of liquid is injected into the
chromatographic column. This liquid carries each component
in a mobile phase from left to right. However, a fraction of
these molecules may stick to the porous media and form the
stationary phase.
A. The Equilibrium Dispersive Model
The EDM is a nonlinear convection-dominated partial
differential equation in the form (1), that governs the ﬂow
of i = 1, . . . , Nc components occupying a chromatographic
column in mobile, ci, and stationary, qi, phase.
The EDM is a mass-balance equation which is based on
six fundamental assumptions. These assumptions are: 1) The

chromatographic process is isothermal, and the transition
between the mobile and stationary phase is modeled by an
equilibrium isotherm with instantaneous reaction kinetics.
2) Band broadening occurs due to axial dispersion. 3) The
chromatographic column is radially homogeneous. 4) The
compressibility of the mobile and stationary phases are
negligible. 5) The mobile and stationary phase of each
component do not interact and 6) the stationary phase is
packed with a porous material of equally sized spherical
particles. A rigorous derivation of the EDM is presented in
[7].
Under the governing assumptions 1) through 6), the EDM
models the multicomponent ﬂow inside the column Ω=

B. Nonlinear Equilibrium Isotherm
The transition from mobile to stationary phase is modeled
by a nonlinear Langmuir isotherm [7]. The reaction kinetics
are assumed inﬁnitely fast, such that the stationary phase
reaches equilibrium instantly. In preparative chromatography,
the equilibrium relationship is typically nonlinear. Therefore
this paper considers the nonlinear Langmuir isotherm given
as

III. NUMERICAL METHOD
The EDM (1) is discretized using a method-of-lines
approach. This allows for a separate discretization of the
temporal and spatial variables. A high-order, DG-FE method
is used to discretize the linear, convective-diffusive ﬂux in
(1a). The spatial discretization leads to a set of ordinary dif-
ferential equations. The solution to these ODEs are advanced
in time by a Low-Storage, Explicit Runge-Kutta Method
(LSERK) and during each iteration the nonlinear system (2)
is solved by Newton’s method to obtain the concentration.
A rigorous derivation of the DG-FE method is presented in
[20]–[22].

relations (2) and (19b) are equivalent. The matrices Ac and
Bc are the discretized ﬂux operators obtained by the DG-FE
discretization in (13). A low-storage, explicit, Runge-Kutta
(LSERK) method of the form
p(0) = x(tn),
(20a)
(
k(i) = aik(i−1) + ∆tf(p(i−1), tn + ci∆t),
p(i) = p(i−1) + bik(i),
(20b)
g(x(tn+1)) = p(5).
(20c)
for i ∈{1, . . . , 5} is used to advance the solution to (19a)
in time. Newton’s method is used to solve (2). The solution
to (2) is the concentration x(tn+1). The coefﬁcients for the
LSERK method are given in [26].
IV. CONTINUOUS-DISCRETE EKF AND ENKF
Consider the continuous-discrete stochastic nonlinear sys-
tem
dg(x(t)) = f(x(t), u(t))dt + σ(t)dω(t),
(21a)
g(x(t)) = x(t) + Fq(x(t)).
(21b)
The inlet concentration, u(t), is modeled as an unknown
input, since measurements typically are not available at the
inlet for industrial feed stocks. The process noise, ω(t),
is modeled as a standard Wiener process with incremental
covariance Idt. Knowledge of the initial state x(t0) ∼
N(x0, P0) is assumed and it is assumed that measurements,
y(tk), of the output, z(tk), are available a discrete times, tk,
at the outlet
z(tk) = h(x(tk)) = Cx(tk),
(22)
y(tk) = z(tk) + vk,
(23)
where C = [0, 0, . . . , 1], because the last element of the state
vector is the concentration at the outlet. The measurement
noise, vk = v(tk), follows a normal distribution vk ∼
N(0, Rk).
A. The Extended Kalman Filter
This section describes the extended Kalman ﬁlter. Assume

initial states given as
ˆx0|−1 = x0,
P0|−1 = P0.

C. Disturbance Modeling
To estimate the unknown input, u(t), a disturbance model
[14], [27]–[29] is proposed, i.e. the model (21) is augmented
with an integrator state, xd(t), modeled as a random walk
dxd(t) = σdωd(t).

V. NUMERICAL EXAMPLE
The numerical case study consists of two parts: 1) The
ﬁrst part compares the performance of the EKF and EnKF
in terms of the CPU-time and root-mean-square-deviation
(RMSD) for varying number of ensemble sizes. 2) The
second part demonstrates the capabilities of the EKF and
EnKF for nonlinear, input estimation.

ensemble sizes. Each dot represents a simulation with a
ﬁxed ensemble size. This comparison considers ensembles
q ∈[2, 3, 4, 8, 20, 40, 60, 80, 100]. The comparison is made
between the root-mean-square-deviation (RMSD) of the state
variable and the total CPU-time for computing the ﬁltered
states. The RMSD is computed as

Figure 2 and TABLE II show that the EKF updates the
estimated states signiﬁcantly faster than the EnKF and able
to produce a reasonably accurate state-estimate in terms of
the RMSD. The EnKF obtains more precise state estimates
for larger ensemble sizes, but consequently also requires
signiﬁcantly more computational time than the EKF. The
computational bottleneck of the EnKF is the propagation
of the equations (42) in the time update. However, these
computations are independent and easily parallelized, which
would lower the computational cost signiﬁcantly without
compromising accuracy.
B. Input Estimation: EKF vs. EnKF
Consider the same column parameters, feed and initial
concentrations as listed in TABLE I. The feed concentration
is now injected after 0.5 minutes with an injection time
of tinj = 7.5 minutes. The feed stream remains active
for the remaining simulation, since tf = 8 minutes. The
polynomial order and elements are chosen as N = 2 and
K = 10. The process and measurement covariances are
Q = ∆t = 0.14 and R = 0.22 when using CFL = 0.3.

measurements only appear at the outlet which produces a
time delay measurements in the estimated states.
VI. CONCLUSION
We have presented a high-order numerical model for ac-
curate simulation and state estimation of equilibrium-based,
nonlinear chromatographic processes. We have developed
the EKF and EnKF for efﬁcient simultaneous estimation of
the uncertain states and inlet concentration based on noisy
measurements at the outlet. The EKF is signiﬁcantly faster
compared to the EnKF but produced less accurate state
estimates.
